THIS PAPER SHOULD BE MENTIONED WHEN WRITING
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
*-®-* li o 13 ulletin,'’
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy,
Tub Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
h. Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
U cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
Ihe direct_ election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
J 5, be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
Ihe Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the dajr. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
Ihe Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailing]y spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin >s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south, of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

SATURDAY, JUNE 11, 1892.
Parkes upon Kanaka Slavery.
The following letter bearing the signature of
Sir Henry Parkes appears in Sydney Daily
Telegraph :
It is a startling commentary on the high-flown boasts of
Australian democracy that at this moment a proposal is
submitted for a conference of these colonies to consider
the question of introducing into Australia inferior and
unprivileged races of men. The Parliaments of two of the
colonies, it is recorded, have already given their sanction
to the dark irruption. And great interests are at stake
which cannot be conserved without cheap and submissive
labour. What does all this mean? Consider it in whatever
light you will, it means something to be done which the
free men of our own race are not competent to do. It
means some necessity, real or imaginary, which can only
be met by the service of aliens who cannot be admitted to
our franchises, who are ignorant of our conditions of life,
and whose bone and muscle can be obtained on a low
commercial scale. Does it, then, mean slavery ? Let us
see.
There are other slaves besides the human creatures who
are stolen from their native soil and sold in a foreign
market to the highest bidder. Slavery itself may be a
slavery of degrees. There may be an absolute slavery ;
there may he a mitigated slavery. But slavery in any
form has no natural place in the life of a free people. The
only foundation and the only security for the freedom of
a people is political equality. The concession to popular
feeling which has been so widely made that the new
traffic shall be hedged round, by special precautions and
safeguards, concedes too much. Special precautions and
safeguards are incompatible with the equal rights of free
men. But however well designed this exceptional code
of regulations may be, who is to ensure its beneficent
enforcement? There must he functionaries of some sort
in the place of the man-stealer, the man-seller and the
man-driver of the old system with the ugly name. No
one will pretend that the best and most tender-hearted
of men will volunteer for these unenviable posts. Let us
suppose a labour ship—l will not say slave ship—at one of
the islands ; there is no electric telegraph, no press, no
judicial court, no constable, no pulpit or platform orator
to overlook or pry into the ship’s doings ; and do we not
know that the labour agent’s sense of right and duty will
largely depend upon temperament, disposition and bias of
feeling ? But the cause is half abandoned which pleads
for tolerance through this litany of pretences.
We are told by Burkr that where there is “a vast
multitude of slaves, those who are free are by far the
most proud and jealous of their freedom.” Hence the
rebellious spirit of Virginia and North and South Carolina
at the beginning of the revolutionary war. Is this the
latent reason of the desire for inferior and unprivileged
races in Australia? But were we to throw open our doors
to the Indian coolie and the benighted islander, would not
our restriction on the Chinese be a grim farce? In one
respect the proposed new traffic would result in a worse
form of real slavery than the open slave trade supplied to
the Southern States of America. The African slavers
planted both sexes on those rich lands by their awful
trade in flesh and blood, and we know that many happy
family groups relieved the tragic gloom of hopeless servi-
tude.* But we should have loathsome clusters and
sprinklings of one sex embittered by the enforced absence
of the other.
In the course of human progress barbarous peoples have
changed their character by slowly winning step by step
their rights and privileges. It is proposed that we should
reverse the order of progress, and barter away our
character as a free people to secure the profitable invest-
ment of ill-directed capital, which even now reserves its
wage fund for aliens. England expended the gigantic
suin of twenty millions sterling io compensate the West
Indian planters for the emancipation of their slaves. Are
we to gather up our resources to initiate a system of
castes and degraded classes, which cannot exist among us
without weakening our free institutions and vitiating our
national character? The application of the word fedora
to any design of this stamp is a mockery and a profana
tion.
* # *
It is noteworthy that the Foreign-trade
press of Sydney has passed this letter by
almost entirely unnoticed. The Telegraph
published it with not one word of comment
or approval. The S. M. Herald and its
mild spectre, the Echo, ignored both the
letter and the writer thereof, and only the
Evening News had the moral courage to
approve of Parkes’ utterances, which it did
without in any sense expressing approval of
Parkes himself. The trail of the National
Association and the Employers’ Union is
over the metropolitan press of Australia, for
these bodies love cheap servile labour above
all things, and the Kanaka is the epitome of
cheap labour in its most servile aspect,
therefore the daily press worships the
Kanaka and his brother the Coolie, and
would gladly sec them spread all over the
Australian provinces. When Parkes was
the prop and mainstay of Foreign-trade
in New South Wales the shoddy press would
have whooped—sadly and reluctantly, no
doubt, but it would have whooped none the
less—for his anti-slavery theories, because in
these days it was necessary to support
Parkes in everything, from his Dead House
to thank troops in down to his garbled
accounts and the shortage in the public
Treasury, for the sake of the cheap calico
cause. But of late Parkes has practically
abandoned the Foreign-trade party, and
there is no longer any necessity to reverence
the ancient fetish, and great is the fall
thereof.
* # *
Whether the venerable politician is any
more sincere than usual in his declamations
against Polynesian slavery no one save him-
self can tell with absolute certainty. Pro-
bably even he could not throw any light upon
the question. It is the curse of party
politics that they make no allowance for
sincerity, and the individual who suffers
from that virtue is almost always a failure.
The business of the politician who is out of
office—under the grand old existent system
of Government by Contradiction—is to be
always, and in everything, hostile to the
Ministry, and the business of the politician
who is in office is to be for everything that
is likely to keep his majority together, and
against everything which threatens to dis-
integrate that majority. As Parkes
has held office longer than almost
any other politician in Australia the
reasonable assumption is that he is a good
deal less sincere than even the average of
his kind. But in the present instance there
is very little to be gained, save a small and
inefficient advertisement, by espousing the
cause of the Polynesian, and therefore it
may be presumed that Parkes’ sympathy
for that miserable islander is a genuine emo-
tion. The Dibbs Cabinet has shown no direct
desire to boom the slave-holding interest, so
that even the gratification of contradicting
the Government is denied him, and the
occasion may be noted as the very first one
on record when Parkes, being in Opposi-
tion, did not contradict the Government.
The Kanaka has no vote, and even if he had
one he has no opportunity of voting for
Parkes. He is so far off that he is never
likely even to hear of Parkes. He has no
money to subscribe to a testimonial, and his
signature- if he ever had one—is of no
earthly service on the back of a promissory-
note. Therefore The Bulletin concludes,
with a certain measure of surprise, that Sir
Henry’s letter is written under a purely
generous impulse. Possibly the writer might
have given way to such impulses more fre-
quently if he had not been a politician and a
K C.M.G., and the nefarious system of
Government by Contradiction had never
been invented.
# • *
Apart from all questions of sincerity, the
ex-Premier’s protest is a forcible denun-
ciation, and if it had been written by the
Parkes of 12 years ago it would have set
New South Wales aflame. The boss kid-
napper, who is nob allowed to work on
Sundays, and the Government Agent, who
is always to wear gold lace on his sleeves and
hat, and the Polynesian inspector, and all
the rest are only the man-stealer and the
man-seller and the man-driver feebly dis-
guised in a varnish of outer respectability.
The elaborate restrictions that are put on
the traffic are themselves, as ex-Premier
Parkes points out, an evidence against it,
for freedom means equality, and the intro-
duction of a race which cannot be placed
upon an equal footing with the other in-
habitants of this continent is slavery, despite
the surface gilding that serves to cover in
some degree the noisome thing underneath.
The Kanaka traffic, under the new regula-
tions which Griffith has imposed, looks
more honest than any honest traffic can
afford to look. The long list of strictly
Christian rules and laws and bye-laws them-
selves give it away, for such extraordinary
restrictions are only applied to things that
are themselves foul or nefarious, and their
existence puts the trade in the same category
with the importation of Chinamen, and the
sly-grog business, and the sale of poisons, and
the pawnbroking industry, and the Leprosy
Act, and the removal of refuse out of back-
yards, and similar morally or physically
unclean works and callings. The existence
of law is in itself a protest against wicked-
ness, and a business which requires so much
more law than usual is obviously more
wicked than the average. The whole
code which Griffith has constructed, right
down to that moral regulation which forbids
any male entering the female quarters on
board ship “ unless accompanied by the
Government agent,” bears witness to thei
unspeakable vileness of the entire system,
and that regulation is itself a fair
sample of the humorous villainy of the
trade, for it only provides, after all, that if
one man goes into the quarters of the female
slaves with a criminal intent, he shall be
accompanied by another man who probably
has a criminal intent likewise, and thus there
shall be two of them instead of one.
« # •
Parkes and Griffith are old enemies, and
their hatred dates from the time when
Griffith took a leading part in the first
great movement for Australian Federation,
while Parkes stood aloof and did his utmost
(as in the original movement for secular edu-
cation, promoted by a political opponent of
his) to bring the project to ruin. Parkes
and Playford— -the coolie-importer of South
Australia—are also enemies for no very dis-
tinct or well-defined reason, though possibly
their mutual dislike arises from the fact that
Playford looks like a bad moral copy
of Parkes himself, and could pass in a
dim light for a damaged bust of that
aged poet executed by an inferior artist with
a cold chisel. Yet it is strikingly un-
characteristic of Parkes that he has missed
the opportunity to heave vituperation at
these two objectionable politicians. He has
generously taken up a matter which holds
forth no visible prospect of any dividend,
and while advocating the cause of the op-
pressed, he has carefully abstained from
throwing strong language at the oppressor in
his own personal interests. Perhaps even
Parkes is becoming gentle and forbearing
in his old age, or it may be that his with-
drawal from the clamour of politics is respon-
sible for the change. Anyhow, the result is
sufficiently surprising to be worthy of notice.
The spectacle of Parkes among the prophets
is an unwonted experience, and even the
oldest inhabitant of New South Wales can
hardly recollect when any such vision was
seen before.
The South’s Duty Towards the Heathen.
The sudden outbursts of virtuous indignation
that, at fitful intervals, seize upon the public,
both in Australia and England, with regard to
the treatment of the aborigines, are wonder-
fully characteristic of the British people. When
the so-called Exeter Hall crowd raise a voice of
protest against the iniquities of Queensland it
may be safely affirmed that it is simply to
balance a little annexation and spoliation going
on in some other quarter of the globe, probably
Africa. The denunciation of the native police
of North Queensland serves to distract atten-
tion from the slaughter of 400 Jebus and the
capture of their barbaric monarch. So far as
the Australias are concerned it is decidedly a
case of the pot calling the kettle black. The
South gets up and says that it is not as the
North is; but if statistics could be taken, as
many blacks in comparison have probably been
shot in New South Wales, Victoria and South
Australia as in Queensland. In the South, the
nigger-shooting has been left for private hands
to do, with the result that, now and again, in a
virtuous spasm, some unfortunate, no whit
guiltier than the others, has been hanged. When
the nigger has been pretty well wiped out, and
we have got all his country, and hopelessly
degraded what is left of him, then we start
aboriginal stations, reserves and missionaries.
* * »
Speaking from a purely practical point of
view, unconnected with any feeling of duty or
humanity, there is no valid reason why the
nigger should not be wiped out by—let us say,
natural decay. Ethnologists have discussed
him over and over again, and decreed that he is
the lowest existent type of humanity ; that the
prognathism of his superior maxillary almost
degenerates into a muzzle; and that the alveolar
arch, instead of being round or oval is nearly
square. Not to mention that his head is
elongated and depressed in the coixmal region,
and his immense orbits are overhung by pon-
derous superciliary ridges. All this ought to
make him sit down and die out of hand, if he
has any pride left in him. But, unfortunately,
he has not ; what little he ever had, we rob
him of at once by treating him as “a black-
fellow.” He accepts the situation and becomes
“a blackfellow ” in the lowest sense of the
word. It is all there is left for him to do.
What we ought to do from motives of duty and
humanity, if we ever are actuated by such feel-
ings, would be to recognise the fact that while,
as a tame blackfellow, he is despicable, as a
savage he is a respectable member of his own
class of society. When the aboriginal is in his
natural wild state he works hard. He
constructs ingenious weapons with tools that
would make a white mechanic scratch his
puzzled head till it was sore. He ornaments
these weapons after his idea of art; and the
first white robber who comes that way
promptly confiscates them, and probably sells
them to a clergyman who is making a little
collection of curios for friends at ’Ome. He is
a good hunter and works hard for his living.
His womenkind make string out of bark and
therewith make kangaroo nets, fishing nets, &c.
They weave shapely baskets out of grass, and
can sew skins together with a bone. The
savage makes the acquaintance of the white
man (after he has been shot at several times)
and straightway becomes “ a blackfellow.” He
lives on offal and scraps, partly because it is
easier to sit down and wait for such to be chucked
to him, partly because the white robber has
dispossessed him, and spoilt his hunting-ground.
If he makes any weapons now, they are miser-
able shoddy things to be sold for rum or tobacco
When he is too lazy to do this, he sells his woman
Then, when he is ready to become a finished
hypocrite, the missionary comes along ami
makes him one. This is the sort of civilmatioi
that is the secret of England’s greatness. 1
* # *
If it could be done, and there is no reason
why it should not be practicable provided
all Australia united on the subject th
aboriginal might be left to live and die !
savage. That is to say, those who are still
fortunate enough to be in that position. \yi
have plenty waste land in Australia to’ spare
for the wants of the original holders of'th e
soil ; and as the unavailable districts are
mostly good hunting country, some could be
reserved from the earth hunger of the present
generation to afford a refuge for a dying
despoiled race. The system of reserves
is, of course, in vogue now to a certain
extent. But the reserves in question should
be conducted on a very different principle
Exclude every white man from the place
save the one or two properly accredited officials
Keep out equally the greedy land-grasper and
the meddling missionary. The experience of a
century has taught us that the aboriginal is
non-receptive so far as permanent improvement
is concerned, and is far better off in his ori»inal
state than as a loafer on the white man, w?th a
yearly dole of a blanket on the birthday of
Kaween Wikitoria. The Maoris, a warlike
and powerful race, fought for an independent
tract of their own, and won it by the strong
hand. The States and Canada have their
Indian territory. The Australians, a weak and
scattered few, have their acres wrested from
them without parley or apology, and are
then abused for degenerating. This, of
course, is a way all nations have* of
serving weaker ones, but what right have
Victoria and New South Wales, which
have finished off their own aboriginal trouble
to bark at a newer province for repeating the
practice they first initiated ? The very jshop
wherein the smug citizen who holds up his
hands in pious horror at the reputed atrocities
of the Queensland people, makes a living, ig
probably erected over the bones of a murdered
aboriginal. No ! the only chance for the native
is to keep him apart from the whites, for our
touch withers, and our breath blights. Give
the remaining natives a few detached areas of
the unoccupied land on our continent whereon
they can live unmolested ; and let them there
die out as decent savages, not amongst ns as
outcast blackfellows. And, above all, do not let
the pious inhabitants of New South Wales go
round throwing stones recklessly at the other
provinces, because if they have no blacks left to
shoot, it is only because their fathers and
grandfathers did all the slaughtering for them.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Globetrotter on Agriculture.
The Earl of Meath, a British nobleman who
has lately been maundering round Australasia
on some undefined religious errand, left a
conundrum behind him at Auckland for the
people of these provinces to solve. It is the
same conundrum which every fuzzy-headed
British pietist does leave behind him, and the
constant repetition of it shows how much utter
drivel and stupidity, and how very little plain
horse-sense, goes to make up a great nation :
The problem is as follows :
What I cannot understand is how, with your vast area
of unoccupied land, there should be any unemployed in
Australia.
That is also what Booth wanted to know, and
David Christie Murray, and Sir George
Campbell, and John el Sullivan, and a
great number of others ; and most of them
also desired to be informed why, with such a
great area of land, Australia can’t take in all
the poor of the United Kingdom, and furnish
them with homes, and thus build up a great
nation. And not one in all the great agglomera-
tion of asses seems to realise the facts of the
case, which are these. (1) The emigrant, in
nine cases out of ten, is not a farmer, therefore
the land is no good to him. (2) He can’t be
turned into a farmer, for agriculture doesn’t
come by instinct. The average Briton always
imagines that any scavenger or decayed sailor
or broken-down shoemaker can farm in Aus-
tralia, though the idea of the same individual
being capable of farming in the waste lands
of England is too ridiculous to be considered.
(3) There is no more an unlimited demand for
agricultural produce than there is for red silk
pocket-handkerchiefs or paper collars, and the
number of producers can’t be indefinitely in-
creased in this line more than any other unless
the consumers are increased also. (4) The
typical emigrant has no money to buy land, and
if Australia takes him in and gives away her
inheritance for his benefit she has next to build
roads and railways to carry his produce, there-
fore he is for many years a dead loss. (5) He has
also no money to build a house, to fence his land,
to buy seed and agricultural implements, and
without these the land is of no more use to him
than is a diving apparatus, or a steel girder. (6)
Avery great part of Australia is of little-
good without irrigation. The emigrant, or the
city “ unemployed,” who is often the same
individual in a later stage of development, has
no money to irrigate with, therefore unless the
Government finds the money he is helpless, and
in any case it would be cheaper to build a big
factory for him and make him a present of it
than to give him a block of land gratis, and
find him in tools, and railway communication,
and artesian water supply, and keep him till he
realises a crop, especially seeing that he seldom
knows anything more about how to realise a
crop than he does about realising an oil-paint-
ing. (7) If Meath or any other visitor were to
go out penniless into the great desert with only
a spade he would understand all these things,
and a great many more which are now crowded
out for want of space.
*.
The Labour Agency Swindle.
At a time like the present, when business is
stagnant and work is scarce, the labour agency
shark flourisheth exceedingly throughout Aus-


tralia. His business is one that requires no
capital, no brains, and very little energy ; all
that is really essential is a small, dirty office,
an utterly callous soul, and a board outside the
door stating that domestic servants, pick-and-
shovel men, and all sorts of other employes are
“ wanted.” Also-, it is advisable now and then
to insert an advertisement in a daily paper,
setting forth that there is a vacancy for a clerk,
and that the hours are short and the salary
liberal and the work exceedingly light. This
draws attention to the office if business shows any
signof fallingoff. Such an advertisement will fetch
at least a hundred applicants, and the labour-
agency man interviews them one at a time, and
is so entirely satisfied with each man’s capa-
bilities and so perfectly sure ho will get the billet,
that the preliminary charge of ss. for an intro-
duction to the alleged employer seems a matter
too small to be worth considering. Then the
applicants interview the “ employer,” who
generally proves to be just such another dirty-
looking man as the agent himself, lurking in
just such another dirty office ; and from him
they leani that the vacancy has, unfortunately,
been filled up exactly an hour earlier. No
one, of course, can absolutely prove that there
never was any vacancy or any successful
candidate, though the fact that no clerical staff
is visible save a pimpled boy reading a cheap
scalp-story in an empty room, seems to point to
that conclusion. No clerk ever does get a billet
through such a labour office, and no employer of
any repute would hire the ordinary, mangy-look-
ing agent as a healthy medium through which to
obtain one, but the drowning “unemployed”
catches at straws, and the ordinary labour-agent
is a straw—and a very unpleasant one at that.
Now and then he finds a job for a few pick-and-
shovel artizans—that is to say he copies the
advertisements in the morning paper, and puts
them on his board, and charges ss. for giving
the address; and if there is nothing doing in
this way he hires them for a mythical billet
somewhere in the bush—so far in the bush that
they seldom get back to commit a justifiable
and well-deserved homicide upon him and all
his family. He also finds genuine jobs in cheap
restaurants where the wages are low and
the work tremendous and the hours 18 per
diem, but in this case the restaurant-keeper
generally sacks his employes every week, and
gets anew lot from the same office, and the ss.
which is paid by each one for a week’s work at
starvation wages is divided between the agent
and the employer. Also, in addition to these
things he often does a little business in domestic
servants —generally for houses of ill-fame. The
Australian is a patient and long-suffering
animal, and though he will at times give way
to great excitement over politics he endures
such swindles as these with hardly a protest.
Yet the remedy is sufficiently obvious. In
the city of New York the labour-agency
fiend is a licensed person, and is liable
to have his license cancelled and his
business shut up if his frauds become
too conspicuous. And for every individual
whose fee he accepts, he must find employment
within one month, or should he fail to do so,
the fee has to be returned in full. If he doesn’t
return the fee then chaos supervenes. The
American law deals tenderly with the swindler
who has taken a million dollars, but for the
labour-office man who has gone off with one
dollar, it has no bowels of compassion what-
soever. The adoption of this system, in all its
naked simplicity, is just what Australia wants
very badly at the present juncture.
Somnolent Victoria.
The hollow bray of asininity is so frequently
heard in the council chamber of Melbourne
Town Hall that the recent belching of Godfrey
Carter, M.L.A., at a little bon voyage function
to the glory of Fitz Gibbon was allowed to pass
unnoticed by a tired outside world. Yet from
the mouth of Carter came a specially poisonous
exhalation right on top of the most pompous
display of wilful ignorance ever uttered by a
“ loyal ” ex-Mayor. Before venting the sinful
suggestion that “ a thoroughly Liberal Govern-
ment, supported by a Liberal majority, might
he guilty of repudiating its financial liabilities,”
a suggestion so absurd that even a hard-shell
Tory like James Service snorted his dissent,
the member for Melbourne city had been more
seriously funny than he intended. In the
first instance, Carter admitted that Victoria
might get rid of its depression the sooner if the
political Houses were closed for a time. Such
little jokes at the expense of the taxpayer are
expected at civic guzzles, and the “repre-
sentative ” audience laughs hoarsely. “ But,”
added the gruesome joker, “as the people
insisted upon having a Parliament and upon
having 95 members talking every night
upon subjects they knew very little about,
he saw no remedy for the present state of
things.” The offensive jocularity pf Carter
lay in his assumption that the Parliament of
Victoria is a favourite institution. Asa matter
of fact, there is nothing really popular in that
portion of the continent, except a horse-race,
and the demand for 95 Members of Parliament is
a monstrous myth. The people of Victoria are
so deplorably indifferent to everything per-
petrated in their name that a Government pro-
posal to double the number of electorates would
be swallowed with scarce a murmur. Parlia-
ment there (as elsewhere, in a less degree)
doesn’t represent the people, seeing that com-
paratively few of them vote for anybody at all,
and most" of those who do go to poll may be
heard scoffing at the man or men they have just
voted for as being the doubtful best of a bad
lot. Not one candidate in ten is earnestly sup-
ported, or expected to justify his election by
the wisdom and purity of his political life.
Carter may be one of the tenth men,
recognised by his Tory constituents as
a reliable mouthpiece of proputty and sworn
enemy of the “masses,” but even his little
section of the country doesn’t insist upon
having him for a member, although he gets
there all the same. By the country at large he
and his 94 colleagues are merely endured. An
increase in the number of electorates made
some few years back was laughed at but per-
mitted to pass, and the sudden doubling of the
95 would, as suggested above, excite no riots.
If, on the other hand, a petition to reduce the
present number of members by one-half were
put before every adult male in Victoria, 95 per
cent, of the voting community would sign their
names with a joyous flourish, and when the
half-mile of document had been laid upon
the table of the House and repudiated
by the alleged representatives of popular
opinion, the community would feebly accept
the situation and doze oft' again. The people of
Victoria regard their Parliament as something
that is and can’t be avoided—and nothing more.
They neither insist upon returning 95 members
nor desire them to talk in the manner supposed
by Godfrey Carter. They express in private
the belief that reckless roguery is the surest in-
troduction to Parliamentary pre-eminence, and
they haven’t any tangible public beliefs whatso-
ever.

The alleged “ withdrawal of capital ” from
Maoriland, which has been vociferously howled
about ever since the democratic Ballance
Cabinet came into office, is still progressing.
The latest trouble comes from the “Trust and
Agency Company of Australasia,” which
threatens to shift £IOO,OOO of cash out of the
province and leave it desolate. The “Trust
and Agency ” concern is a prosperous institution
whose dividends range as high as 20 per cent. —
they have been higher on more than one
occasion, but 20 is high enough, anyhow. The
people of Maoriland have been blessed for
many years with the joyous privilege of paying
that 20 per cent, on their end or the business,
and now they are informed that, unless the
absentee taxes imposed by the Ballance Go-
vernment are removed, the privilege will be
withdrawn. That is about what the trouble
amounts to. When a man pawns his watch,
and the pawnbroker charges him 80 per cent,
on the money advanced—that is the investment
of capital. And when the man redeems his
watch, and stops the awful accumulation of
interest, that is the locking-up of capital—the
pawnbroker’s capital. And then, if the pawn-
broker can’t find anybody else to put his watch
up the spout and pay the 80 per cent., that
is the final withdrawal of capital, and the
results, according to the Tory press, are ruin
and inevitable stagnation.

fares Minister of Railways Wheeler “ concedes ”

One of the best places in London to pick
up an Australian is Lobb’s (the bootmaker) in
Regent-street. John, who has a “swagger”
business and is now facile princeps THE bootmaker
of London, is very rich.
The Melbourne Tramway Company have
decided to reduce fares on their lines. A dozen
tickets, which formerly cost 2s. 9d., are now sold
at 2s. Gs. Children under seven were formerly
carried for half price ; the age is now raised to 12.
At latest advices the Beagle had been sold
at Callao (Peru), and the Sydney defaulters, Bell
and Davis, are supposed to have vanished into
the South American continent. The chances of
their capture would now appear to be pretty
limited, and most likely the defaulters are about
stone-broke in any case. The successful bolter
generally spends all the plunder in getting away,
and after a brief, hilarious time he gets a billet as
scavenger in some foreign city, or hires out as a
shepherd, or takes tickets at the door of a theatre.
When Yictoria’s land-boom was rosiest,
Speaker Tom Bent, as head of a syndicate, grabbed
the Korongah estate, near Belfast, of nearly 4000
acres, at £2G per acre. Of course, the land was
sub-divided into small farms, but as only a few
fetched £3O per acre the rest were withdrawn.
Since then the land has been leased for agricul-
tural purposes. The other day a frantic effort
was made to sell 1000 acres, and 580 were disposed
of. Of these 100 averaged £3O each, and the
others £25. The worst of the remainder may
fetch a fiver at auction, and the syndicate drops
heavily. Who would fill a Speaker’s Chair that is
stuffed with such thorns as these? j
Dear Bulletin,—A short while back a
man was blown into eternity while blasting rock
at Glenbrook (N.S.W.). His mates quizzed around
for his remnants, and took what they could find
down to his tent, where it was decided to have a
wake. One of their number carved out an artistic
cross ; floral tributes from sympathetic friends
showered in abundantly, candles were placed at
liberal intervals round the bier, a cask of stagger-
juice was put on tap, and all night long the com-
plaining morepork was chilled into silence by the
most awful wailing, and drinking, and other
sorrow that imagination could picture. By the
early train the parents of the dead man arrived,
and no sooner did they review the remains, and
the candles, the tobacco ashes, the tail ends of the
feast, the demolished spirit keg, and things in
general than it struck them like a horse-kick that
a mistake had been made—a real Erin-green wake
had been held over a red-hot Orange Protestant.
What followed was too painful for description,
but it will be along time before another Protestant
gets a wake from the wild Irishmen of the Blue
Mountains.—Yours, &c., Zero.
Captain Subritzsky, of theschoonerMedora,
has captured the sea-serpent and towed the
carcase triumphantly into Auckland (M.L.). The
animal was sighted off Gape Brett, and the
captain gave chase and fought 16 rounds with his
enemy till at last he stunned it with a tremendous
blow on the nose and then strangled it. It is
about six feet long, eight feet in circumference,
and is possessed of four flappers. The front pair
measure close on eight feet from tip to tip, and
the weight of the body is estimated at between
seven and eight cwt. The carcase is completely
encased in shell, both above and below. That on
the back is shaped something like a shield and is
ribbed and fluted, the shell being splashed with
white spots on the dark ground. The head is
fish-like in shape, and about 9 or 10 inches long. It
is provided with eyes and spout holes, but
apparently is without ears. Upon opening the
mouth a formidable array of teeth are discovered.
What might be termed the terminal of the under
jaw outward, is a sharp parrot-like tooth, and on
the upper jaw are a couple of similar ones to match.
Inside both the mouth and throat is one mass of
teeth about an inch in length. The neck is
remarkably thick, and the creature was able to
raise its head about 18 inches when fighting or
swimming. From which it would appear that the
sea-serpent is the son and heir of the old fish-
lizard. Possibly his father is still lying low at
the bottom of the sea waiting events, and when
he comes up trouble will eventuate.
The Eddy-Schey Commission.
Scene : Sydney Equity Court—a Court designed
after Chief Justice Harley’s own heart —(that is to
say, there is about one-eighth of the room avail-
able for the public, and the rest is sacred to the
noble profession of the Law). There is a small,
low gallery at one end which constitutes the only
place reserved for the people, and into it they are
jammed as tightly as possible. If any ventured
to stray within the sacred precincts of the lawyers
they would probably be put to death for “con-
tempt.” In the middle and along the sides of the
Court are the seats - sacred to the legal fraternity.
These gentry are not engaged in the case —they
are merely looking on. Large numbers are pre-
sent ; evidently having nothing to do in their
offices they come here to pass the time. There
are more of them round the walls and leaning
against the mantelpiece. To the right of the
bench the reporters are grafting away like demons
with the printers’ devils waiting for copy. Why
do printers’ devils always look bored and discon-
solate ? If a printer's devil went to a pantomime
for copy he wouldn’t look at the pantomime. He
would lean against something and stare at the
floor and look tired. Perhaps they are born
tired. In front of the bench is a big table
where the dramatis personal sit. There is the
heavy villain, Mr. Schey, a fat man with a flat
forehead, straight nose, sharp eyes somewhat sunk
in the fatness of his cheeks, his mouth and chin
covered by a stubby, hay-coloured beard. There
is the injured hero, Mr. Eddy.
Eddy is a disappointment. One had
expected a large, dictatorial, autocratic, over-
bearing sort of personage, with big eye-
brows, bristly beard, and a hoarse voice.
He is a large man certainly, but flabby
and soft-looking ; wears a vast frock-coat, which
flaps loose upon him like a giant’s robe upon a
thievish dwarf; has a somewhat feeble, smug
countenance, soft beard, and lank, straight hair.
Very like a lay preacher of the Methodist per-
suasion, or a shop-walker in a ladies’ drapery
establishment. Beside him sits Oliver, one of his
brother Commissioners. Oliver is a modified
edition of Jack Want—bristly eyebrows, aggres-
sive moustache, fighting under-jaw. He is a
fighter from the ground up, is Oliver; has been
so all through his career. When he was in
the Lands Office, in the flush times of free
selection, things got into a muddle. The Survey
Department couldn’t keep pace with the work.
The Lands Department got behind-hand also.
Oliver came to the front from a subordinate posi-
tion. He worked night and day and posted him-
self up in everything. When the Minister wanted
information he could never get it from anyone
except Oliver. He knew more about the Survey
Department than the Survey officials themselves.
He had to fight the bitter enmity of various moss-
grown placemen, but he came through it all and
secured the Under Secretaryship of Lands, which
meant practical control of the whole Lands Office.
Thence he went to the Railway Commissionership,
and has thereby got about as high in the service of
the country as a non-professional man can get. At
the table also sit the minor characters of the play
—M'Lachlan, Secretary for Railways, a tough
and energetic customer with a long memory
and no nerves ; Smith, Attorney for the
Commissioners, a sharp-faced man with hair
standing straight up like quills upon the
fretful what’s - his - name. On Mr. Schey’s
behalf appears Crick, M.L.A., champion mud-
slinger of the House, the only man Parkes is
afraid to tackle in an interchange of epithet. If
Parkes called Crick a withered tarantula Crick
would probably call Parkes a “lop-eared some-
thing with the morals,” &c., &c. So Parkes and
others let Crick alone.
* * •
On the Bench sit the Royal Commissioners
—Owen (Judge in Equity), John Young, and
Alexander Kethel. Judge Owen, in his wdg and
gown, is about the most imposing-looking Judge
in N.S. Wales. But upon that Bench, without
his hairy ornament, he is not so imposing-looking
as his two colleagues, both unlettered men com-
pared to him. There is a rugged dignity and a
practical common sense about them which inspires
much respect. The questions they put are always
terse and to the point. John Young argues with
Schey and corners him with great skill.
# # *
The proceedings are little more than a
farce. Mr. Schey, after having in the House
charged the Railway Commissioners with every-
thing from pitch-and-toss to manslaughter, has
suddenly “withdrawn from the affair” and left
the Royal Commissioners to do what they think
best. They are paid to find out whether there is
any truth in the charges. With this object they
put Mr. Schey in the box and question him. He
is quite at his ease, and fences with the President
with great skill. He will give no definite inform-
ation. He believes his charges were true. People
told him certain things and he believed them and
made the charges. He won’t give their names.
Ho won’t say on oath that his charges were
true, but is of opinion they were. So the affair
goeson from oneoharge to another, Somemisguided
people have an idea that Mr. Schey is a very deaf
and helpless person who finds himself in an un-
pleasant predicament and heartily wishes himself
out of it. Not at all. He is quite at his ease and
gives the Commission a Roland for their Oliver
every now and again. For instance, the President
starts at him as follows —“ You say, Mr. Schey,
that the Railway Commissioners were guilty of
ignorance or incompetence or something worse.”
Mr. Schey—“I meant what I said—something
worse.” “ Yes, but there are many things worse
than ignorance or incompetence—what was the
particular thing you meant? ” “ Well, the words
are ordinary English. I said something worse,
and I meant something worse.” “ Yes, but what
worse thing did you mean?” “I meant some-
thing not so good.’’ “ Then the Commission under-
stands that you meant nothing in particular.” “ I
am not responsible for what the Commission
chooses to understand.” Judge Owen, hotly;
“No, but you are responsible for what you say,
sir 1” “ Oh yes, and I said—‘something worse !’ ”
* # #
A very few minutes in the room convinces
any unprejudiced person that if Mr. Schey has
any evidence to offer he has every chance of offer-
ing it. Some people think he was blocked from
putting his case by his ignorance of technical
matters. In truth, the whole aim of the Commis-
sion is directed to getting him to say something
definite —anything that can afford any informa-
tion. But he has “washed his hands of the
matter,” and is too artful to give any assistance to
the Commission which will either prove or dis-
prove the charges. Whether he can wash his
hands of the responsibility of having made a
violent attack upon people who, so far as any
pro f to the contrary brought by him is con-
cerned, have apparently been serving the country
to the best of their ability, is another matter.
# # *
To all those who believe in socialism as
against individualism, the success of the N.S.W.
State-owned and State-managed railways is of the
greatest importance. If this management fails,
then the State will fail also to manage other and
more delicate undertakings. And when three
men are chosen to take charge of this great
national undertaking, it is bad that the whole
system should be endangered by the attacks of a
man who throws mud from behind the fence of
parliamentary privilege and then afterwards
“washes his hands of the whole affair,” hoping
that some of the mud will stick on the men whom
he has attacked.
* * *
If Australia cannot manage her railways she
will have to lease or sell them, and thus hand
herself over to the tender mercies of railway-
kings and monopolies. And we will never get first-
class men to manage our railways if they are to be
subject to abuse and misrepresentation without
any redress. The railway servants who demand the
abolition of the Commissioners would find them-
selves in a very much worse plight if they were
the servants of a company who would begin by
sacking half of them, and go on to work the other
half to death at low wages.

Dear Bulletin, — Let me tell you of
Paddy Miller’s ball. Some time back the Tip-
perary boy who carted wood to our (S.A.) boring
camp called upon us one evening and invited all
hands to his daughter’s birthday ball; you bet, we
went. On arrival at Paddy’s chateau, we found
the ballroom —size, 12ft. by 13ft.—crowded —■
packed, in fact. The orchestra, consisting of two
accordeons, a flute, and a tin whistle, were seated
in the ample fireplace, and a five-gallon
wicker jar filled with beer right in the
centre of the ballroom was the pivot around
which the backblocks youth and beauty
radiated. After a while, the heat of the room
having become intolerable, I asked our host if I
might open the window. “ ’Deed, thin, yez can’t,
sor,” replied Patrick, “but if it’s ventilation yer
want —Mary, pull out the corks ! ” My attention
just then was called to the flowing bowl, and I
naturally thought Paddy’s remarks had reference
to beer. In a little while, however, I noticed now
and then a low, moaning, weird sound, a sort of
accompaniment to the polka which was being
played by the band, and as the dancers were warm-
ing up to their work, and the beer beginning to tell,
I mentioned the subject of air again to the lord of
the castle. “ Musha, thin, sor, but ye’re the divil
for ventilation. Shure Mary pulled out all the
corks on the aist side, an’ if the corks on the wesht
side is pulled out, begob the bhlast that ’ill come
through will blow the gurls’ ball-toggery over their
heads, so it will.” The ingenious Paddy, finding
ordinary windows too expensive, had knocked the
bottoms out of a score or more of bottles and had
them built (neck-inwards) into the mud of which
his shanty was built. —J.O.R.
This story is told of the late “ Ted” Lee
of N.S.W. who was looked upon as an infallible
tipster by his old school-mates, even though many-
a-one of them had had bitter experience to the
contrary. At the time of the Champion race at
Randwick when Ben Bolt was beaten by Zoe, Lee
was supposed to know all about it. On the day of
the race Ranny Want came to him in the paddock
and said : “Eb, old man, I know you know all
about Ben and Zoe—which do you think will
win?” “Well, Ran, old chap,” said Lee, work-
ing away at his finger-nails, “ I don’t see how the
old horse is to be beat.” This was enough for
Want, who immediately invested with Charlie
York the few pounds he had in his pocket, at
whatever price that not too liberal sportsman
would lay. After the race, which was won by
Zoe, Want was coming down the steps from the
Grand Stand to the paddock, looking, and feeling,
very crest-fallen, and just in front of him was Lee
with his arm on a friend’s shoulder, and gleefully
saying ; “ I pipped it right that time and have won
a couple of hundred ; I knew the old horse had no
chance.” Want rushed up to him when they got
down stairs and said : “ What’s that I hear you
say ? I thought you told me the old horse couldn’t
be beat, and now you tell David Bell that you
knew he had no chance.” “ Well, Ran, old man,
I know I did. I’m very sorry, but you're such a
damnable sieve." Those who know Want’s pro-
verbial propensity to “ blab ” will appreciate this
story.
Cornhill describes the scene at the trial
of Parson Clutterbuck, the Church of England
preacher, who swindled confiding old women by
promising to invest their money in Government
securities at 10 per cent.: —
In the court below stood the prisoner’s father, a white-
faced old man, like a bad bust of Vespasian, with a blank,
glazed eye, and the cords of his throat straining out of
his coarse red muffler. Sometimes he turns without
emotion or curiosity to look at his offspring, who now
throws back the cape of his coat dramatically, and stands
with arms folded, and now fidgets with the yellow rails of
the dock, with a hand that is as plainly bad as his face.
. . . . Four years’ penal servitude—and the convict
bows and bends himself away like a beaten hound. And
out of court shuffles, still quite emotionless, the old
father, in a long drab ulster, and the few hairs standing
straight above his curious round head, with its flat top.
It is a strikingly familiar scene. When some
particularly bright and shining saint is on
trial for a specially mean swindle, unless he is an
orphan, it is an even chance that there is just such
another blank-eyed, flat-headed old man maunder-
ing in the court and watching the result. If the
grandfather is there too—whi ch doesn’t of ten happen
—the three make a study in heredity. We saw such
a trio once. The son —in the dock —had a sugar-
loaf cranium, and a weak chin, and a shifty eye.
The father, shuffling about aimlessly in the body
of the court, had a skull that was perfectly flat,
and on top of it one hair stood up in wild con-
fusion. And doddering in a corner was the aged
grandfather, with a long, horse-like countenance
that was utterly expressionless except that he
looked as if he wanted some oats. Once he got on
his feet suddenly, and we instinctively said
“ woa !” It would have been worth a lot to have
had the great-grandfather along with the group,
but he was dead. Someone showed us his tomb-
stone afterwards though, and if there ever was a
tombstone that was calculated to be put over a
bull-dog that was the one. We formulated a
maxim out of that family group, and this is it:
“Never trust the son of a flat-headed father, or
the grandson of a man with a horse-shaped
countenance.” If you do you will assuredly be
left, especially if the grandson has a head with a
peak at the top.

My Dear Moorabinda, —
The only things I didn’t like at Walter Bentley’s
“ Hamlet ” were mere trifles. Watt-Tanner, as the Queen,
hadn’t enough tails to her stage ermine to duly impress
the circle, and Laura Hansen brought iu a very unsuit-
able collection from the face of nature for her “larded
with sweet flowers” scene. “Here’s heart’s ease ” said
she, handing a lanky jonquil to Laertes. By the way,
Air. Bentley’s “Hamlet.” isn’t extensively Bowdleriscd,
and when an actor gives Shakespeare his head the
innocent d.c. maiden passes most of her time directing a
blank stare at Hie roof. At one passage of the tragedy I
thought some of the darlings would crick their little
necks.
Woman, you know, ought always to study to be a tiling
of beauty, hut she can’t do it if she goes cycling. And I
am afraid she will, for at a recent meeting of the Sydney
Bicycle Club four ladies were admitted to membership.
Now, I have seen a Sydney she-bicyclist in going-order,
and I have a fatally clear vision of half-an-inch of white
embroidery hanging below a narrow skirt which had
worked up all round, revealing lace boots, black ho —
but I mustn’t proceed. She didn’t look a bit happy,
and she kept on making dives at her skirt to pull it down,
but it wouldn’t pull, and her male escort seemed to want
the earth to open and swallow him, so did she when the
embroidery fluttered more than usual. If Sydney girls
intend to take the air on wheels instead of legs, they must
follow the lead of a certain cycliste who, I hear, gets over
the dress bother by adopting man’s attire, and local
belledom, when it orders round its fiery tricycle, must
leave its goffered jupons behind, too, and come out boldly
in the divided garment—there is really no help for it. Of
course, the girl with knock-knees may consider the effect
of Turkish trousers, and the seraph with nice ankles may
be allowed to discover that knickerbockers and stockings
are ever so much cooler—but the ground-plan, as it were,
must be forked or else the modest youth will look another
way when a lady cyclist turns a breezy corner. But,
however you arrange matters, a woman astraddle is a
Hideous Object.
There’s a lot of nonsense talked and written about
“ mother’s love,” and .-lie shriek of the whole army of
matrons if a drunken woman has her howling infant taken
from her by the law is, in my opinion, another audible
reason why we shouldn’t have petticoats voting. In a
recent (Southern) divorce-case a tipsy, unfaithful wife was
forcibly deprived of her neglected olive-branch, which was
handed over in court to the father, a seemingly decent
person, who promised that she should have access to it
now and again—and one would think that the respectable
Australian matron would have been delighted at the
rescue of the youngster from so depraved a “parient”—
but, no. “ A Mother ” looms in public print lamenting
that the divorced female (who had hysterics in court, of
course) wasn’t given time for “one last kiss—one parting
embrace,” &c., and trusted (with asperity) that future
petitioning husbands might be possessed of a little more
“ milk of human kindness.” A feminine judge, I sup-
pose, would have allowed the maudlin mamma to shed
whisky-inspired tears down the infant’s pinafore while the
co-respondent went out and bought more cake and ham
for it; also, legal business would have been obstructed
while the poor mother sadly curled up Tommy's hair on a
door-key for the last time. But it hardens one’s sympa-
thetic soul to remember that the infant was there to be
idolised all along, and yet the lady preferred the cordials
—and the co respondent; though it is extremely likely,
mind you, that some other woman will discover that the
poor thing’s great, motherly, broken heart was probably
big enough for all three.
I can’t help wondering if, when he and she are legally
torn asunder, there is no “sorry I spoke” feeling on her
part, especially when he is rich ? As an instance I know
of one woman in a Southern city who foolishly discarded
her old man because he was too sweet on the inevitable
governess. The law kindly awarded her the family (some
lanky girls and a hungry hoy crying out for education)
and she swaggered out of court with them—but after a
while, as the sum her ex-hub. paid her wasn’t large, and
she had to live in a little house, and worry to make both
ends meet, divorce didn’t seem such a happy release after
all. Hubby married the bone of contention, 100, who
flaunted in and out of the gilded gates in Hie carriage
“ that once was mine,” and, to add to the miseries, the
lanky girls had to “go out” governess! nsr, and heaped
reproaches on her head for reducing them to a state of
indigence when they might have been in satiety; and the
hungry hoy always fixed her with a reproachful glare as
he went to his little ten-and-sixpenny billet of a morning
It is no doubt a womanly thing to shunt the wicked old
man when he runs off Hie marital track, but it isn’t strict
business, and though a woman may easily relinquish the
sentimental, she must never lose grip of Hie material.
For what is the im of the wedded Angelina beggaring
herself and her children while the fair co-respondent is
elevated into her place ? It is kind of her to eliminate
herself and give the other woman a show, of course, hut
she is a lovely idiot if she does. And looking round at the
state of this and that household it becomes apparent that
she doesn’t do it renj often nowadays.
A Mrs. Somebody advertised in a daily the other day for
“a governess, not elderly,” ami I was quite puzzled ut
this extraordinary mark of confidence in Mr, Somebody
until I accidentally discovered that the advertiser was a
widow.
“ Masks and Faces ” was the fleeting show at the Royal
on the glorious first of June, and the artistic Mrs. B.R.
waved three very satisfactory garments at a representa-
tive circle which included Mayor and Mayoress Manning
and ex Mayor and Mrs. Burdekin. Thelatter brought a large
party with them. Mrs. Beere’s first staggerer was a
inadeira-eoloured velvet, trained Directory coat with
Watteau back and iappets of gold galon over a yellow silk
jape ; hat of velvet with yellow plumes, and a white cane
with bouquets of daffodils completing the costume. In
the second act, Peg wore a vermillion-red velvet sleeveless
coat over an Empire front of silk cn unite edged round the
hem with gold tassels, the Empire waist dclined by gold
netting. In the third act, Isabel “ Urkert” (she shouldn’t
paint her lips so rose-hudishly in the middle —when the
crimson goes up to the nostril it looks as if the lady were
trying to lick the tip of her nose) wore a very charming
costume of cream silk with floral stripe in blue and pink
and flounces of white lace—hut it was altogether Loo
modern. The semi-Watteau pleat lined with pink was a
sop to the century, hut it had nothing to back it up,
except the lady’s handsome mantle of peach-pink brocade
with sleeves and dorsal stripe of moss-green plush, which
was just the sort of thing a person’s great-grandmother
(if she had one) used to sport. Mrs. Deere’s garment in
tiie last act was a stiff saoque of copper brocade over a
petticoat of yellow satin veiled in cent lace, red shoes
which showed off well in the sand-jig that Peggy (I fell in
love with her Irish brogue, by the way) danced with her
arms a-kimbo. And if 1 had space I would make a little
fuss over Marius’ white velvet brocade suit with gold
tassels and rutiles which—if the combination of colours
did rather remind one of ship’s cabins—was altogether too
fetching when taken in conjunction with the coach-and-
horses patch that drove furiously over Sin jin’s ex-hus-
-1 and’s wicked left eye.
One difference between the Deere and (lie Porter—l
mean the Potter—is that while Deere’s rich draperies are
subordinate to the lady herself, Potter’s rags used to rise
up, as it were, and strike you violently in the eye. The
lovely Cora only wanted a squeak and a sawdust interior
to be a model doll.
And, talking of dress, actresses, and their ways—is it a
rule in the society dramatic world for actresses (whoso
stage plumage is gall and wormwood to every would-be
smart woman in the house) to get up like guys in the
street? For they all do. It has always puzzled me why
women, who are jealous when hubby unwisely enthuses
over the star’s toilet, don’t manage to let him see her by
daylight? The best-dressed women on the stage are
invariably the worst-dressed off—untidy slops and baggy
veils until you say, “ It can’t he she ! ” or, “it isn’t her!”
But it is “her ” invariably.
The inaugural ball in aid of the Fresh Air League took
place in the Centennial Hall on 30th ult., and was a
financial and social success, thanks to the generalship of
Dr. and Mrs. Mailler Kendall, who were leading spirits in
the affair. It’s too post mortem to detail the pretty-
dresses sported from Lady Jersey back again, but I cannot
forget one girl who sat out a dance or two on the cold,
marble staircase, with her swain one frozen step below'
her. A girl who can canoodle on a marble slab must have
surprisingly warm sentiments to keep her from feeling
like frozen mutton after ten seconds’ dalliance —don’t you
think so? The Fresh Air ball (it’s to be annual, by the
way) will echo down the corridors of time as the first ball
from which an all-wise committee made quite a tidy sum
by selling look-on-from-the-gallery tickets at a florin each.
School of Industry ball is to be plain and fancy this
year, and will eventuate at the Centennial Hall on 291 h
inst. Government House will be well represented on the
dais, and it is to be hoped that so deserving a charity—l
mean the School of Industry—will be benefited by a
bumper attendance.
Cardinal Moran moves with the times-die sanctions a
hall in aid of that excellent charity, Tempi) Refuge—and
has even throw n out a suggestion that a committee of
gentlemen lie formed to supplement the usual ladies’
ditto. So the ball is to be held, probably during July*
Formerly his Eminence (who is not a waltzing prelate) is
understood to have looked rather coldly on dancing
.charity-schemes, hut it’s just possible we may yet see
the red hat rotating to three-quarter time.
In the recent impersonating-an M.IVs-wife case it was
reported Hut the principal titled lady-witness (the
victim) concluded one portion of her evidence by saying;
“The accused, after cordially shaking hands with me,
departed.” Now-, it is my solemn conviction that if the
accused had curbed her longing to claw her dupe in
parting she wouldn’t be where she is now. Your average
knight’s wife laughs contemptuously at the nervous
impostress who ventures no further than the hall, and
tells her cock-and-bull story with awe and deference—
she is simply told to “ go along, my good woman ; we
don’t want no more of this,” by the flunkey, and there
are no police and no “ persecutions,” because she humbly
tried to work her poor, little, miserable swindle from the
kitchen, and not from the drawing-room standpoint, and*
as nobody’s amour propre suffers by it, she gets off scot
free. But the w'ell-pluckcd trickstress, who bounces into
my lady’s boudoir—poses easily on its sacred furniture as
she unwinds her plausible narrative, and ends her
infamous proceedings by actually shaking hands with her
victim !—well, when she is found out she hasn't the
shadow of a chance it the victim catches her. It isn’t
only the swindle that has to be avenged, it’s also the
“ howdy-do.” The charge-sheet (or whatever it is) ought
to he inscribed “crime aggravated by prisoner shaking
hands with Lady Blank.”
The funniest thing at N.S.W.’Art Society’s annual
conversazione this year was the stupid way in which Percy
Spence's pictured joke, “My Sweetheart when a Boy,”
was taken by the assembled folks. Spence drew a rococo
sort of lady—just the kind of middie-aged syren whom
small boys feel ready to die for—hut can you believe that
nobody saw it? Not even the daily papers next morning.
The enfant terrible is always cropping up, and the very
latest sample is a southern doctor’s very small son, who
borrows his father’s tall hat and goes about by himself
paying calls. He visited the bishop recently, and after a
formal reception got on to the lawn with the house-chil-
dren, with whom lie inaugurated anew game—standing
on their heads with his father’s heaver! Government
House came next on his list, and Lady Blank received him
and offered him refreshments, w hich he politely accepted,
and after munching through a cake naively remarked,
“ Ma’s a big story she says she never gets anything fit
to eat at Government House !” A chi Id like that would
have to he chained up if (here was a marriage in the
family.

At Sydney Garrick, on Saturday last, Mr.
Walter Bentley made his first bow to a Sydney
audience in the character of Hamlet. The - latest
representative of the Danish philosopher, whom
tlie daily press is at present engaged iu persistently
criticising as the ‘prince,” is a clean-cut, well-
built, admirably-proportioned man, with an easy
carriage, a good.stage presence, a bluff delivery,
and an intelligent, if not absolutely intellectual,
tout ensemble. There is sometimes a burr, or hard-
ness, in liis voice, reminiscent of the larboard
watch ahoy, but, taking him for all and all, he is
a worthier representative of Denmark’s high-born
pessimist than Sydney has had the good fortune
of becoming acquainted with-for a very long time.
1 lis version of “ Hamlet ” runs on the old, time-
honoured lines—with some new cuts—and he
appears to prefer a “ handsaw ”to a “ hernshaw,”
and a suit of “ sable” to a suit of “ scarlet.” He
moreover, neglects the “ mole ”in the ghost-scene
on the rampants of Elsinore, he omits the
“whale” and the “camel” in the Polonius
bantering, and the suggestive raillery in which
the Shakespearean original indulged with Rosen-
crantz and Guildenstern anent the stops of the
recorders, and a deal of other dear old familiar
business that cannot possibly be crowded into
three hours. Perhaps the play acts all the better
for the elisions, but custom hath made them in us
a property of easiness, and the hand of little
employment hath the daintier sense. One cut,
however, occasions to our mind some little con-
fusion—the throwing out of the scenes wherein
the banishment of Hamlet to England is rendered
emphatic, and hence, his re-appearance in the
grave-yard a necessary link in the logic of events
that lead up to the final catastrophe of the fifth
act. But one cannot
have everything, even in
Shakespearean drama.
* * #

At Sydney Royal one night last week
(Wednesday is becoming the fashionable “first
night” now) Mrs. Bernard-Bcero appeared as the
fascinating Irish hoyden actress, Peg Woffington,
in the brilliant old-style comedy in three acts, by
Tom Taylor and Charles Roade, yclept “ Masks
and Faces” —considerably adapted from the novel
of the last-mentioned collaborateur, and named
after the histrionic toast of old-time London. The
play, of course, belongs neither to the age of
Queen Anne nor to that of Queen Victoria, and it
takes a deal of illusion to make one swallow
it —after Congreve and the rest—ns a genuine
picture of Woffington’s dear old delirious days.
It has an occasional habit of reminding one
touchingly of “Tom Jones” with the warmth
cooled a.way by the bellows of Buchanan hut it
is, nathless, a good acting comedy, and it affords
a lot of scope for a real artiste like Mrs. Bccrc.
The drama in question appeals to the heart
which in romantic conrdy generally means the
green lushness of almost culpable inexperience ; in
Peg Woffington's case it m -aus wilful blindness,
which ultimately occasions her a bad heart ache,
and several people a deal of embarrassment. There
is, however, a lot of genuine pathos in “Masks
and. Faces,” and this, backed up by plum-eoloumi
coats with lappels, and the knee-breeches and
powdered wigs of a past age, draws much emotion
from the recalcitrant eyes of the cultivated peopß
who gather, on {esthetic considerations bent,
well-dressed circle on a select first night.
* » #
As the clover, refined, tricky, frolicsome,
humorous and sympathetic Irish actress, Peg
Woffington, Mrs. Beerc lias achieved her first pro-
nounced artistic triumph in Sydney. Whether
she is bandying witticisms with her Bohemian
comrades, exchanging love vows with Ernest
Vane, hurling sarcasm at Pomander, or pouring
out words of hope and encouragement to the
poetic family of Triplet, she is a dulcet joy and a
dream of everlasting delight. She is the play, the
whole play, and nothing but the play ; everybody
else could readily be dispensed with, except
Triplet—the pathetic old poet and painter and
woe-begone tragedian, whose thunders provoke
laughter and whose comedies move to tears. In
the hands of Mr. Herbert Standing, Triplet is a
delightfully natural character—Grub-street rc-
divivuis, with a voice whose every'tone and quaver
is a memory of Dr. Johnson and Chattertou
and Goldsmith and Savage; cow-heel for
supper, and a straw - bed for twopence in
a literary cellar. Mr, Marius plays the
part of Sir Charles Pomander. Possibly he
plays it well, but he makes Sir Charles appear to
have lived so long in Paris as to have forgotten
his native tongue, and to have transferred the
onus of expressing his thoughts to his eye-brows
and his shoulders. He looks well, dresses well—
but he is, nevertheless, M. Marius. As a Russian
police-magnate the Marius is colossal ; as an Eng-
lish buck of the Georgian period he is decidedly
out of it. Mr. J. F. Catheart ro-appears before a
Sydney audience in the character of Colley
Cibber ; and, good actor as Mr. Catheart is, he
cannot make us believe that Colley C. “ gang after
that gait.” Mr. Frank Emery as Snarl, Mr. Otho
Stuart as a pretty edition of Ernest Vane, Air.
Kemp as Quinn, and Mr. Guy Staunton as
Colander, assisted in many more or less material
directions to fill up more or less important in-
terstices in the piece, and the scenery was adequate
to the requirements thereof. In the character of the
neglected wife, Miss Isabelle Urquhart was a
pathetic vision of cream-clad loveliness, but for a
country-bred damsel she appeared far too elocu-
tionary. Miss Watson had a brilliant little pait
as Kitty Clive; and many other people fulfilled
jhe requirements of the minor characters of the
play with suffi.ient success to cause no serious
hitch. On Wednesday last “ Fedora ” succeeded
“ Masks and Faces,” of which a notice will appear
in next issue.

“ New Lamps for Old,” produced at Mel-
bourne Bijou on Saturday, is a social earthquake
in three acts, resulting in a peaceful resurrection
of the belief that marriage isn’t a failure. Far
above the crash of shattered ideals and the shriek
of a family solicitor dropping down, a well-hole
can be heard the voice of the author poking borak
at Mrs. Mona Caird, and advising young married
couples not to elope until they have found some-
thing rational to elope with. “New Lamps for
Old ” is not so much a hundred of bricks heaved
at Ibsen as a reckless destruction of a doctrine
that Mrs. Caird didn’t preach. J. K. Jerome, in
fact, supposes an impossible set of lunatics and
holds their madness up to nature in one of the
funniest farcical comedies that ever defied criticism.
The average elopement in stage married life is a
sad and sinful proceeding, the wages of which is a
long-drawn-out dissolution to the melancholy music
of muffled fiddles. Ou this occasion the husband
flies with a remorseless female who drags him along
by the collar whilst the wife leaves her happy
home for the sake of along-haired poet, and before
they have all started to honeymoon at the same
rural pub., something occurs to send them trotting
individually back to the place from which they
came. By which time they have discovered that
the double-barrelled elopement was a ghastly error
of judgment, inasmuch as the advanced divinity,
who intended to take the married man for a month
on trial, was still hungering for the poet who
wandered off with the wife, these twin invaders
of domestic peace having previously tried a sort
of spiritual eonnubiality for a month, and being
prepared to renew the contract. The common or
garden kind of married couple also perceive the
folly of not eloping with one another when they
feel disposed to fly, and the poet’s long hair turns
out to he a wig, and everything comes right with
the exception of the butler, who is deplorably
drunk at the finish, and the family solicitor, who
degenerates into a mark for universal scorn.
* * »
So involved is the absurdity, and so subtle
the best humour of Jerome’s dialogue that a first
performance of “New Lamps for Old” could
scarcely be expected to approach perfection, yet
the company of seven made an all-round success
on Saturday. Mr. Brough, as “ the great Postle-
thwaite,” managed to sink his own identity in the
character for a large portion of the time, and
Miss Ida Herbert, recently back from England,
played her original part of the strong-minded
Ootavia, with much vigour. Mrs. Brough and
Mr. Cecil Ward were sufficiently bright as the
pair of temporary lunatics, the lady, in particular,
warming to her work as she went along. The
’Kiah of “Uncles and Aunts” appeared again in
Mr. Cannam’s grotesque caricature of the family
solicitor, also Mr. Cambourne did well in the
capacity of old Jorkins, a butler who submits to
be “squared ” with cheerful resignation. The show
is put on in 8.8. style, and the vision of Cannain
travelling up and down the second act in the
dinner lift will be engraven on the heart* l of the
Bijou-going public for evermore.

The Italian operatists have completed a
triumphant run of “ Faust ’ at the Princess’s
without sounding any the worse for the strain on
their voices. Tenor Cuttioa even upset all antici-
pations by singing into form again, and has seem-
ingly found a gargle to soothe his larynx and
lubricate his upper register. On Wednesday, the
flow of music was to be maintained by a grand
concert, in which Miss Colbourne Baber and Alan
Morris upheld British interests, and this novel
experiment will be repeated on Friday. To-night
(Thursday) is set apart for another performance
of “ The Barber,” a brilliant success in the first
instance, and “Rigoletto,” with Melossi and
Cuttica at their strongest, may be ready by
Saturday.
** * *
“ Master and Man,” the story of a bad
man purified by not having to pass through the
furnace, was revived at Melbourne Royal on
Saturday. The Bulletin sat at the feet of this
powerful drama and poured its blessing on
Beelzebub, the bull-dog, only a few weeks back,
when Bland Holt & Co. were making the welkin
vibrate in Sydney. There is nothing left to say
about the solid excellence of “ Master and Man ”
as an exhibition of acting, scenery, stage-manage-
ment and canine sagacity. The show is Good,
with a gigantic G. There are flies above the stage
but none on the performance which has mellowed
and gained in strength since Beelzebub made his
Melbourne debut at the Alexandra. Mrs. Bland
Holt is a brighter ray of sunshine than ever, and
others too numerous to be sugared in detail are as
they were, or even more so. Charles Glenny gets
inside the misanthropic hunchback as easily ns he
slipped into the magnanimous tar, and the leading
lacly is never twice alike. One night she is Kate
Bishop and the next she is Edith Blande. Busi-
ness deserves to be big at the Royal where
“Master and Man,” by the way, must be seen
quickly or not at all. It will retire immediately
at the sound of “ The Trumpet Call,” they say.
** * *
The Australian equivalent for “chappie”
continues to storm the Opera House in large
quantities, and the deah girls gather in their
vegetable tributes with much success. “Faust
Up to Date ” would seem to fill that moss-grown
vacuum, the good old “ long-felt want,” therefore
the individual who finds little delight in the latest
edition of the Gaiety Company can retire into the
background and pray for Divine assistance until
he finds more. The persevering Lonnen intro-
duced what was generally accepted .as a brilliant
idea on Saturday. After Alice Leamar had given
them “Ta-ra-ra,” &c., the gentleman bounded on
in skirts and sang two more verses, one of which
sparkled with the suggestion that the audience
“ought to see me booze.” This parody, though
certainly cheap and not very chaste in design, was
so.evidently suited to all classes present that
imagination can’t picture the result of a similar
performance by “Lady” Munro. A fortune is
sticking out in her ladyship for any hall that will
take the hint. “ Faust Up to Date ”is sentenced
to another week, and will do it easily.
** * *
Mr. Dainpiev’s lease of Melbourne Alex-
andra expires this week. The company are pre-
paring for a long march into the interior, and the
gods cast tear-dimmed sheep’s-eyes at Lily, as at a
loved one about to be called away. On Saturday
last the farewell season was inaugurated with a
serio-comic Irish drama, “Help One Another.’
The warm-hearted young patriot loomed large as
usual, the wicked squire gave further proofs of his
everlasting infamy, and the noble-hearted colleen
asserted herself to great advantage. Likewise,
the grand old priest was there to remind them of
their immortal sowls, and the performance, led by
Dampier in person, gave satisfaction to all.
“ Help One Another ” may wind up the season,
but it is to be hoped that an old favourite won’t
farewell in too much of a hurry, or forget to revive
himself in an assortment of popular characters

Brisbane play-goers have had an oppor-
tunity of seeing how the new man Lauri shapes in
some of Elton’s principal parts. Last week the
Royal Comic Opera crowd put up “Dorothy”
and “lolanthe” with “Charity Begins at Home”
as an after-piece. In the former Lauri played
Lurcher, and in “Charity” &e. he essayed Joe.
In both lie gave a dead copy of the immortal and
agile William of pious memory ; but the new man
pans out too much fat to the ton to be original.
Up to date there is but one Elton, and Lauri is
not his prophet, “lolanthe” seems to grow in
grace as the years go by, and Violet Varley is a
gladsome vision of a mother whose alleged age
tots up some 200 years or so. Elsie Cameron,
through sickness, had to be replaced by Mabel
Mac Kay, as the Fairy Quetn. For a wonder the
brass band which brings on the effete upper house
was in good form, and business has been good.
Brisbane is not such a ruined community as it
looks to the casual eye.
