Miss Laura Hausen, whose portrait appears
in this issue, is a young lady who has had a
successful career in England, for several years
past, during which time she has played—under
the Messrs. Gattis’ management—all the leading
feminine parts in “Harbour Lights,” “Union
Jack,” and other dramas. Her greatest success
was in the graceful character of Dora Vane in the
“Harbour Lights,” which she played for over
18 months in the English provinces and
in London. She later on appeared for 12
months at the London Garrick in the com-
pany of Mr. John Hare, taking the lead-
ing part in “A Pair of Spectacles.” At latest
Miss Hansen, after a long and prosperous tour
through Australasia with Mr. Walter Bentley’s
company, was playing Ophelia to that gentleman’s
Hamlet in Sydney.
At Poverty Point.
When old Joe Tolano ran the Lyceum in York-street,
Sydney, years ago, he was one evening, “ ash vos hish
cushtom,” standing at the dress-circle entrance to welcome
the coming guest. To his intense amazement a smart
carriage drove up, from which alighted two ladies of the
high ton. They speedily found they had made a mistake,
were bound for the Masonic Hall, where the
oratorio “Judas Maccabteus” was being performed, and
they accordingly bowled off again. Joe was relating the
incident a few minutes afterwards to George Morton, who
said in his usual dry way, “ A very natural mistake, Joe.
They saw you at the door, and took you for Judas.”
Janet Aohurch and her hubby (says our London'corres-
pondent) must be dropping money fast at the Avenue,
w'here they are playing “A Doll’s House.” There won’t
be much of the profits of the Australian tour left at the
end of 12 months, if the thing lasts so long.
When-Max O’Rell arrived in Melbourne, he received a
letter complaining of the title of his lecture “The Scotch
at Home.” The writer said that his countrymen refused
to acknowledge that name, which was merely an English
word, and used in a contemptuous sense. If Max didn’t
alter that name, no Scot would attend his lecture. He
altered it.
Billy Jones, of Fitzgerald’s Circus, is a wonderful mam
Surely he must have been digging down nigh half a cen-
tury, this Falstafflan, a splendid example of the health
and respectability of circus life ; and Professor Miller
daily grows more and more like him. Jones is a mine of
circus lore about Burton, Taylor, Rowe, and all those
people.
Frank Oates, we regret to hear, is laid up in the Alfred
Hospital, Melbourne, where he lately underwent an
operation. He was to have played in the “Country Girl,”
but his health compelled him to resign from the service of
B. &B. He has had an offer for the new venture at
Sydney Opera House on 18th inst., but it is doubtful
whether he will then have sufficiently recovered.
“ I hear from very good authority,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “ that Sir Arthur Sullivan is not likely to
recover from bis present illness. He may possibly get
better for a time, but it will be only a flicker of life’s
candle, which is nearly burned out. The clever Montagu
Williams is also seriously ill, and little hopes are enter-
tained of his recovery.”

road a Bulletin notice in which his son was not credited
with a double strain of white blood. Heaven only knows
why the Ounnamulla gentleman objected to the mistake.
White people are a drug in the market, whilst aboriginals
are so precious that we have societies for preserving them.

A coaching item from the history of Kyrle Bellew, as
told by Mrs. Brown-PotterThere have been many
instances of strange misfortune, and no end of heart-
breaking contrasts in this world—Marie Antoinette on the
scaffold, Beau Brummel in a Boulogne garret, Napoleon
on St. Helena, and Rutherford B. Hayes raising chickens
in Fremont, Ohio, for example—but it is to be doubted if
the world in all its changeful history has known so
pathetic an instance of calamitous reversal of circum-
stances as that related in London the other day by Mrs.
James Brown-Potter, when confiding her experiences in
South Africa to an agitated reporter. One afternoon in
Capetown the theatre was burned down, and with it a
good portion of Mrs. Potter’s wardrobe and all of Mr.
Kyrle Bellew’s. “As a rule,” said the lady, her own fine
eyes moistening at the recollection, “ I don’t like to see a
man cry, but my heart went out to Kyrle as he stood and
watched his c'o'.hes go, while the big tears rolled down
•his cheeks.” Rugged, indeed, must have been the heart,
either of woman or man, which could have witnessed that
scene unmoved. But terribly tragic as was this situation,
it Jbecame by comparison mere comedy to that which
followed when the manager—a gross, commercial brute,
with a soul not above the box-office—hired a hall and
instructed the company to go on the improvised stage in
their ordinary clothes, everybody’s stage rags having
gone up in smoke. Mr. Bellew’s role forbade trousers
absolutely—he must wear tights or the dramatic unities
would be dynamited. The manager, troglodyte as he
was, had not the hardihood to approach Mr. Bellew with
the proposition which suggested itself to his coarse
mind; so he went to Mrs. Potter and proposed—
as she phrased it in her own delicate way to the
reporter—that “Kyrle might be accommodated with
a pair of tights improvised from men’s underclothing
stretched and dyed.” She behaved like a real
lady, for, to continue her chaste narrative: —“ I decided
not to wound Mr. Belfew’s feelings by repeating this pro-
position, but on the following day the manager insisted
that Mr. Bellew and the other members of the company
should provide themselves with gentlemen’s underwear
instead of tights, and resume playing. When this pro-
position was made to Mr. Bellew, he rejected it with
indignation and horror.” The management went so far in
attempting compromise as to offer to pay for Mr. Bellew’s
underclothing, but, backed by the now thoroughly
aroused Mrs. Potter, he refused, and they left. Thus was
a notable service to history rendered, and the dignity of
the dramatic profession maintained. For many hearts
would have ached had Kyrle Bellew appeared on the
stage in an outfit of South African gent’s underwear
—Kyrle Bellew, who but a few years ago turned
the heads of whole battalions of New York’s
most intelligent and modest women of society,
who sought to give to his delicate and fascinating
manly beauty woman’s ennobling and refining influence
by hanging around the stage-door to get a glance from
him as he came out of the theatre. And Mrs. James
Brown-Potter, too—how could she have looked upon the
underwear and lived ?—this one-time flower of Baltimore’s
and New York’s drawing-rooms, who, after making the
acquaintance of “’Ostler Joe” and the brandy of noto-
riety, acquired a distaste for the lemonade of social
triumphs, and forsook all for art and Kyrle Bellew, in-
cluding her husband and child. But how unromantic a
Romeo would Kyrle have made, sighing beneath his
Juliet’s balcony in pajamas!— Argonaut.
Schumann, when in the first stages of insanity, was
haunted by the sound of one particular note, which rang
in his ears night and day till it drove him into utter
lunacy. His Widow, who was, till a few weeks ago,
principal professor of the pianoforte at Frankfort Con-
servatorium, is now haunted by the same note. She
resigned her position in the hope of getting away from it,
but it has followed her into private life, and unless she
can get rid of it the chances are that she will go the way
of her husband.
Countess Russell, the hysterical young person who sued
for a divorce and got badly left, has appeared in a
comedietta—“ Sweethearts !”—at London Royalty and
made an object of herself. The Countess, by the way,
lately committed the ghastly error of trying to dance on
the stage, an impossible feat for any matured amateur.
Mrs. Dick Russell, sister to the noble “star,” also
appeared and participated in the family failure.

It is now quite evident that the N.S.W.
Public Works Committee should be elected by
open voting. Frankenstein, the elector, must be
allowed to see how his monster works in every
detail. The Works Committee elections by secret
ballot have up to date been fruitful in sectarian
and other paltry conspiracies and in organised
breaches of faith, and at least one man has been
elected for whom it is safe to say many of his
supporters would not have dai'ed to vote openly.
Dear Bulletin :—lsn’t it about time that
the country shut down on that canting politician
who always states that the thing he doesn’t
approve of is “ inimical to the best interests of the
country?” If this phrase could be buried, along
with the “ right-thinking person ” who is always
dragged in as evidence when the “Constitution”
requires to be propped up, the nation would feel
a great deal better. Yours &c.— Wrong-thinking
Person.
History repeats itself in every direction.
Any captain who brings Chinese to Australia is
responsible for the .£lOO a head poll-tax in the
event of their landing unauthorisedly. The other
day a couple of Chinese firemen escaped from a
French vessel at Newcastle, and it is alleged that
the captain, who went in pursuit and ostensibly
recovered them, merely caught a couple of other
Chinamen and induced them to temporarily fill
the places of the deserters. This recalls the story
of the N.Q. policeman whose two Chinese prisoners
escaped him, and who calmly went and handcuffed
a couple of other yellow men, and brought them
along to the lock-up.
Commissioner Eddy is an energetic,
nervous, sensitive, irritable man who thinks
quickly and too much, and lies awake o’ nights,
and gets nasty when he’s over-worried—and when
a man has two commissions enquiring into his con-
duct, and a couple of railway-accidents to settle
up, and a big railway system to manage, and
arrears of work accumulating all the time, he is
apt to feel that he has on his shoulders a bigger
load than he can bear for long. Mr. Eddy should
studiously avoid crossing swords with his critics
and enemies in public, and should leave that busi-
ness to a fat man like Commissioner Fehon, who
can swear genially, and who, as he has no “ per-
sonal dignity ” and doesn’t dress too carefully,
or in an English professional manner, is privi-
leged to tell Australian people of all sorts,
without offence, exactly what he thinks of them in
a bluff, to-the-devil-with-you sort of way. Mr.
Hoyle, as a fat man without gall, should have a
great advantage over Commissioner Eddy in
public encounters, and in the recent trouble he
failed to utilise his opportunities.

Last Saturday morning that unlucky man,
Thomas Walker, M.L.A., was drunk in a railway-
carriage at Sydney, While in that condition he
seems to have started fumbling with a revolver,
and in the course of his wobblings, it would
appear, the weapon went off and shot) Rev. David
Laseron in the back. The owner of the revolver
apparently didn’t even know that his weapon had
gone off, and sat gazing at it in a fatuous manner
until he was arrested, when he woke up and
“scrapped” promiscuously with everybody. As
Walker is a Protectionist, and has fought some
tough battles in the labour-interest, the Sydney
Daily Telegraph reports the case as follows :
An unprecedented shooting affray took place in a rail-
way carriage as it was passing through the yards at
lledfern early on Saturday afternoon, in which Mr. Thomas
Walker, M.P. for Northumberland, shot at and severely
wounded the Rev. David Laseron, incumbent of St.
George’s Anglican Church, Glenmore-road, Paddington.
The victim now lies at the Prince Alfred Hospital with
the bullet embedded in the fleshy part of the hack, below
the left shoulder-bl d while his assailant is confined at
the Redfern police station.
This, on the face of it, appears to be an utterly
unfair and malicious attempt to create a prejudice
against Walker. Firstly, there was less “affray ”
in the matter than there was when Sir Henry
Parkes fell out of a cab and broke his leg.
Secondly, it has not been shown that Walker
“shot at” Mr. Laseron; the evidence so far all
got s to show that Walker was absolutely intoxi-
cated, and didn’t even know that he was shooting.
Thirdly, he was not an “assailant,” but a drunk.
Thomas Walker is also one of the most unfor-
tunate drunks going. It was just like his evil
luck to pot a preacher at the first shot, for his
criticisms of the clergy have been so rough that
half the community will take it for granted that,
though he may not have intended to shoot the
reverend gentleman, yet, had he made up his
mind to wipe out somebody, he would have
enthusiastically selected a parson as his target.
The Rev. Mr. Laseron had not, at time of writing,
got rid of the bullet.
The new home of the Victorian Artists
Society, Albert-street, Melbourne, is somewhat
suggestive of the brilliant tourist suit in which
Mr. Dorritt, junr., started to “do” Switzerland.
The universe, according to Charles Dickens,
wasn’t capable of providing travel commensurate
with the requirements of young Dorritt’s costume.
Victoria doesn’t yet produce art sufficient to come
up to that imposing gallery—albeit, the winter
exhibition of 300 odd works in oil and water-
colour, supported by a small collection of statuary,
cannot be honestly sneezed at. The most preten-
tious and popular picture on view is “ The Death
of Burke,” by Arthur Loureiro. This is a big
subject on a big canvas. Wills, gaunt, emaciated,
but not pathetic in his misery, sits at the feet of
the dead Burke in the foreground of a crushed-
strawberry desert. The picture doesn’t appal an
ordinary spectator, and it is easier to see paint
than to feel nature in the general effect. Douglas
Richardson contributes several productions of the
cranky school, intended, doubtless, to be lofty in
conception, yet suggesting nothing beneath the
unlovely surface. For example, “ A Nereid ” is
the back-view of a scraggy, squatting, nude figure
who holds out a garfish to a flock of flying sea-
gulls. A strip of impossibly yellow beach and
another strip of pea-green sea are laid across the
floor of this work. There is no health in it.
“ The Kiss of Death,” by the same artist, strikes
one as being a soulless imitation of a strong idea,
and the stupid face of the bloated infant who is
supposed to be passing lienee excites ridicule any-
way. Charming little landscapes and mountain
scenes, by J. Mather—pictures that carry their
own atmosphere about with them, and persuade
the observer that he is out of town—are plentiful,
and specially attractive at their moderate cata-
logue prices. J. F. Paterson’s “Winter on the
Murray Flats ” must please everybody by its
artistic dealing with nature in a gloomy mood.
“Oh, the Dreary, Dreary Moorland!” (Louis
Grier) is, on the other hand, a picture in which
nobody outside the profession can see more than a
5 x4ft. expanse of grey and yellow agony. You
can’t swear to the Moorland, or the clouds, or to
anything except the dreary coating of paint. An
incongruous picture by Catani shows much pretty
work and mixed ambition. A bold-faced female,
with next to nodings on, flaunts her excellent
flesh tints against a dark forest background
calculated to set the young woman off to advant-
age. Two black swans, in a refreshing piece of
winding stream, have sailed up to the bank on
which she stands and seem to be wondering what
this essentially Melbourne nymph does in the
wilds of W. A. Catani calls her a Prophetess out
of an old legend, but the swans know better. She
ought to dress herself and go back to the bar. She
is a nice girl—a little out of balance and at vari-
ance with the canons of art, no doubt, but quite
real and enchanting when studied in little bits at
a time. Arthur Streeton lias some slap-dash
works in which it is hard to find the excellence at
a glance; some oil and water-colour portraits by
Tennyson Cole are fully up to his established
reputation, also “ A Creek of the Ovens ” (J. C.
Waite) calls for special mention in a notice that
doesn’t profess to be more than casual. G. R.
Ashton has a capital, easy standing figure of a
man-o’-warsman reading The Bulletin on the
gun-deck, a picture at once strong as regards the
bronzed sailor and unsatisfactory in much of the
colouring of the accessories. Entitled “Articles
of War” it is commendably peaceful in purpose.
The Jingo spirit finds vent in “ Rumours of War ”
by A. Colquhoun. This is a deplorable wooden-
looking picture of a sailor reading out a cable lie
from the ’Erald, the scene being laid apparently
in the back parlour of a pub. A mounted rifle-
man looks over the sailor’s shoulder, whilst a
valiant volunteer sits on the table and thirsts for
gore. Meantime a girl is bringing long beers on a
tray in the distance. The production is injurious
in sentiment and ought to be turned face to the
wall.

POLITICAL POINTS
“ If the N.S.W. Government die, they will
die gamely, and come up smiling.”—Minister
Slattery.
Taxpayer : If we don’t get pensions our-
selves, why should we subscribe for pensions for
other people ?
Dr. Cockburn has been dropped by some
of the S.A. Oppositionists, who are anxious to get
him out of the way by making him Speaker.
The Adelaide branch of the Australian
Natives’ Association is moribund. Politics and
everything lively are interdicted at its meetings.
Dear Bulletin, — You ask: “Has any
Irish-Australasian- politician, barring Ballance,
ever refused a title?” Yes—Peter Lalor.— Anon.
J. M. Stuart, S.A. Crown Prosecutor, was
once a policeman, and formed one of Inspector
Tolmer’s famous gold escort in the old Bendigo
days.
The real reason of Parkes’ anti-slavery
letter probably was that the N.S.W. Ministry’s
special organ apologises for the Kanaka-catching
business.
The Dibbs Government is now known as
the “ Country Government Mr. Barton, Q.C.,
being the only member representing a metropolitan
constituency.
Grahamßerry’sfirstspeech in the Assembly
was dead against Speight and Co. He hinted
darkly at paying them compensation, and the
Assembly listened hungrily for more, but Berry
was silent suddenly.
A certain Sydney daily always mixes its
Freetrade and Anti-Unionist opinions with the
news it furnishes to iuterprovincial papers in Mel-
bourne and Adelaide.
The whirligig of time brings its revenges.
Parkes now righteously denounces the cheap-
imported-nigger system. But who was it im-
ported cheap half-nigger compositors?
Though Germans control the voting in
half-a-dozen double-member constituencies there
are only three Germans in the S.A. Parliament,
one of them being the Attorney-General.
The first measure passed in the old Parlia-
ment of Canterbury (M.L.) when it met in 1853,
was a humble document providing that the pro-
vince should borrow not more than £2OO for three
months at eight per cent.
Parkes’letter “Parleying with Slavery”
appeared in Sydney Daily Telegraph alone.
From which it is quite evident that the old man
did not send it to the S. M. Herald, a pro-
slavery organ, by the way.
The working-men suburbs of Melbourne
did not roll up well this year with the electoral
suffrage shilling—which is not surprising, for they
have been practically disenfranchised in gloho by
the Specification of Rolls Act.
In Toohoy v. Melville the N.S.W. Full
Court has decided that the chairman of com-
mittees has power to order a member to be
removed from the chamber even without a pre-
vious resolution of the House to that effect.
If Chief Justice Lilley should decide to
cast aside his robes of office it is more than likely
that Sam. Griffith will be invited (by himself) to
occupy the vacant seat. And if such a thing
comes to pass, how Judge Harding will snort!
Baker, M.L.A., claims consideration for
the dethroned Speight from the Victorian As-
sembly as “Britishers, fathers of families, and as
men.” This is aggravating ! When are we going
to be Australians, and have some virtues of our
own to swear by ?
Kingston, until lately the chief S.A.
democrat, now sports a Windsor uniform and says
nice things about the Governor. Once he proudly
stalked round in the scarlet of a sergeant of
militia and hurled invectives against Kintore
from his place in Parliament.
Land monopoly! There are in little
Tasmania 170 private properties of over 5000
acres, the aggregate being 1,981,220 acres. The
acreage of one property alone reaches 199,000,
that of another 142,000, whilst the average for 146
owners is something like 10,000 acres each !
Cabled that the Orangemen of Ireland are
preparing to rebel in the event of Home Rule.
They threatened the same when Catholic Emanci-
pation and Church Disestablishment were coming,
but quietly accepted the inevitable. Nowadays
there is more bark than bite about King Billy.
Another try for Women’s Suffrage will be
made in South Australia this session, and it is
sure to come with next Parliament. The Lower
House in 1890 passed the Bill by the requisite
absolute majority, but in the Council, although it
got the votes of exactly half the members, another
vote was wanted.
The N.S.W. Ministry lost the Bogan
election, which resulted in the return of Squatter
A’Beckett, apparently through their indisposition
to support Labour-member O’Halloran, on the
ground that he was not sufficiently high-toned.
The Ministry are erring dreadfully in this matter
of tone —so think many of the best Protectionists.
( Ihe Australasian Governments move the
Colonial Office to take the prerogative of mercy
out of the hands of the Governor and place it in
those of tho_ Executive Council for the time
being. This is an outcome of the Mount Rennie
case—of the hard hearted soft-headedness of the
“ downy ” Governor Carington, who allowed four
untried but convicted boys to be strung up in a
row.
The proposal of the Playford Government
to refer the coolie labour question to an Australian
Conference confirms recent rumours that they
want to back out of the job to which the blunder-

Several members of S.A. Parliament are
interested in a big land-grant railway scheme
which is to unite Oodnadatta with Pine Creek.
The distance to be traversed is 1000 miles, and the
country to be grabbed can be counted by millions
of acres. Parsons, once Government Resident of
the Northern Territory, is very anxious to get
Parliamentary consent to the undertaking, as also
to the introduction of niggers into the Territory.

The British man-of-war. Goldfinch, has
gone to the Chatham Islands with the fell purpose
of collecting dog-tax. For several years past, the
Maoris at the Chathams have assumed a martial
attitude and declined to pay, and now the Gold-
finch is going either to get the money or bombard
the dogs. All the dogs in the archipelago
wouldn’t pay the cost of the expedition, but the
dignity of the empire must bo kept up, no matter
what the bill amounts to.
“ If Foreign-trade were totally blocked to-
morrow,” says the Brisbane Worker, “ there
would be sweating in Victoria to-morrow night,
and don’t let the Victorians forget it.” Quite
true. And if Protection were abolished to-
morrow the result would be exactly the same,
also if the Chinese were wiped out this afternoon
there would probably be alligators in the northern
rivers before evening. There is no earthly con-
nection between tariffs and sweating, anyhow.
Here is a significant commentary on the
unblushing Foreign-trade theory that a Protective
duty is merely so much added to the price to the
consumer, so that a 25 percent, tariff on an article
costing Is. at once raises the price to Is. 3d. In
Victoria, imported oats, by reason of the heavy
duty, cost 3s. 4d., yet the price of Victorian oats
is only 2-i. 4d. Imported barley, which also
carries a heavy duty, costs ss. 9d. landed in Mel-
bourne, but the Victorian farmer, instead of
running his' price up to ss. 9d., only charges
3s. lOd. Imported maize would cost 4s. GJd., and
yet Victorian maize is sold for 2s. lOd. ; and
potatoes could not be imported under £l3 a ton,
but the local artiple sells for 20s. or 30s. All the
same, Sydney Daily Telegraph will come out
to-morrow with the same haggard wail about the
way Protection adds 25 per cent, to the price of
provisions, and its readers will probably believe
the statement. If you say a thing often enough,
you can always get someone to believe it.
Parkes’ lament that the whole of th e
N.S.W. Ministry are “deserters from Freetrade,”
reminds The Bulletin that the chief cause of
Parkes’ political decadence is the division for the
first time of the N.S.W. Assembly into two real
political and not merely personal factions. If
Parkes had sat on the fiscal fence he would have
been powerful to the last—would have been able
at the critical moment to step down, as he had
ever done, and join the stronger side. But his
’Ome prejudices and traditions were too strong
for him.
The composition of the Ballance (M.L.)
Cabinet does not exalt the Englishman as
a politician. The Premier is an Irish-
man ; Attorney-General Buckley is also Irish;
M‘Kenzie, Minister of Lands, is Scotch; Ward,
Postmaster-General; Reeves, Minister of Educa-
tion ; and Cadman are all three Maorilanders; and
Seddon, Minister of Works, is the only English-
man in the crowd. It is the same everywhere.
The leading Englishmen are almost all Scotch and
Irish, and they keep getting more Scotch and
more Irish every day.
The Griffith Government has decided that
the old case-hardened slavers who acted as
Government Agents in the Kanaka trade in former
days shall have the first appointments, now that the
industry has been revived. This is melancholy.
The traffic was to be washed and boiled and made
clean, but this digging up of the ancient ruffians
most of whom cared for no rules or regulations—
who lived riotously among the Polynesian girls,
and drank furiously when on board, and had
delirium tremens when on shore, and whose whole
career was one long burst of unmitigated black-
guardism, is a promising beginning.
Sydney D.T. published last Monday an
interview with a wandering American—seemingly
of no special importance—named Wallace F.
Kirk, and tried very hard to dig out from him
some remarks favourable to Foreign-trade. These
are what it got. (1) Harrison (the Republican and
cast-iron Protectionist nominee) will undoubtedly
beat Cleveland (Democrat, with alleged Foreign-
trade leanings) for the Presidency. (2) The Re-
publicans are followers of M‘Kinley. (3) Under
Harrison’s administration (when Protection has
been more rigid than it ever was before) the
country has been “ exceptionally prosperous ” —in
fact it has “never been more prosperous than
now.” But all the same (despite the certain
triumph of the ultra-Protectionist party afid the
prosperity which has accompanied their policy)
* ‘ the wiser heads desire a tariff for revenue purposes
only ” (which is another name for Freetrade) and
“ the next Congress will insist on an entire
revision of the tariff ”in that direction. What a
hash !

The Canterbury (M.L.) Times man muses
about Deeming just before the execution :—
And where is Deeming all this time? Most likely writing
a history, full of lies and piety. I would not at all be
surprised at hearing that he repented of his iniquity at
last. Burgess, of the brutal Sullivan gang, did so. I
remember pitching the book into the fire, and so sending
so much nauseating cant up the chimney. It was he who
took poor little old Jimmy Battle into the bush and
choked him for threepence. I heard that Burgess went to
Heaven afterwards. I do not know where poor Battle
went to. Burgess gave him no time to repent. Nor did
Deeming give any of his victims any time to make their
peace with their Maker. I have heard numbers of persons
say that hanging was too good for Deeming. And so it is
—if there be no place of eternal torment. But if there be
such a place, what gluttons for punishment must those
people be who would like to see him dancing on a hot
griddle every day for months before he is hung 1 No one,
really, seems able to say whether there is or is not such a
place as the region alluded to. I never hear the locality
referred to at all now, except by the Salvation Army. One
would like to know whether it is still existent, or whether
it is to be regarded as a bank of clouds, very heavy and
threatening in the morning, but dissipated by the noon-
day ? I went into the kitchen just now to light my pipe.
The girl there, I found, had fastened a supplement of the
Canterbury Times containing views of Deeming’s resi-
dence at Rainhill, and other illustrations connected with
the man, to the wall. She stood before the picture and
was examining it attentively. I asked her what she was
doing. She said she was wondering at it. “Wondering
about what? ” I asked. “ Wondering,” said she, “ how a
man could do such things—and him having such a
beautiful villa to live in! ”
The Mechanism of Man.
A man has within him a working pump called his
heart, a working; bellows called his lungs, a work-
ing vat called his stomach, a working condenser
called his brain, and a working evaporator called
his skin, all of which must be at work whether he
will or not. The heart is expending over his
body day by day, 122 foot-tons of work. He will
go on lifting so many millions of tens in so many
years. If he meet with no accident, the time will
come when his last stroke will be finished and he
will die, or he may be carried off in earlier life by
disease, which, however slight in its early stages,
works like a ferment. The symptoms may be
scarcely noticeable—a little laziness, slight head-
aches or backaches—but the cause is always at
work. Take a handsome, rosy-cheeked young
man, full of life and hope, let a germ of disease enter
his system, it is invisible, too small to be weighed;
judged by the evidence of the senses it is too
insignificant to be thought of, hut it extends in-
definitely. It may be months, it may be years,
but if the first symptoms are neglected the deadly
disorder will do its fatal work ; the step loses its
elasticity, the eyes become dull, the cheeks pale,
strength departs, and eventually the joyous youth
is but a shell, a cadaverous, shrunken form, and
death ends the dreadful scene. With such facts
staring us daily in the face how careful ought we
to be that the first warning voice of nature should
be heeded, and that when we suffer those tired,
aching feelings, nervous headaches, neuralgia, and
other signs of approaching catastrophe we should
have trust in that great regenerator of vital
strength, Clements Tonic, which positively restores
pristine health to every organ of the body, as
instanced in the following case: “In justice to
the value of Clements Tonic I wish to add my
testimony to its efficacy. My daughter has for
several years been afflicted with torturing head-
aches and complete prostration, for which she
tried numerous remedies to no purpose. At
length a friend sent her a bottle of Clements
Tonic, and a few doses gave such relief that she_
will hear of no other remedy; and other persons in'
my household, who have been similarly afflicted,
have the same faith in its properties. W. E.
Murtagh, Mitta Mitta, Vic.” The above was
declared before me at Mitta Mitta. in the North-
ern Bailiewick of Victoria, this 2nd day of October,
1891, John Conway, J.P.

Tennyson is infuriated at a certain Mr.
Collins, who charges him with plagiarism. “ Such
writers as this,” he says, “are the lice on the
locks of literature.” Also, we presume, they are
the flea down the back of poetry, and the bug in
the shirt of art. Tennyson when in a rage is neat
but not gaudy.

Sir Robert Stout, according to a Maoriland
paper, is an incurable lollie-chewer, and never
consumes less than three or four pounds of con-
fectionery per week. Just here we see a visionary
picture of the great barrister rising to open a case
with a large lump of stick-jaw in his teeth and a
damp sugar-stick held in his massive right hand.
Holder, leader of S.A. Opposition, is a
long, cadaverous personage, with a wall eye and a
sidelong gait, who always wears a long black
frock-coat, and glides rather than walks. He has
been a newspaper proprietor, he preached for the
Wesleyans, and he is not wealthy. Holder appears
to have no decided views on any political subject.

Melbournian; When Berry was at the
zenith of his power as Premier, he was taken ill,
through the terrible Black Wednesday strain. A
Melbourne paper sent me to interview him, then
living in Murray Ross’ house Commercial-road,
Prahran. Mrs. Berry carefully intercepted me,
and I could only see the great man in another
room, as he sat dejected, doing nothing, with his
head resting on his hand at the table. The other
day I saw Lady Berry, after all this lapse of
years, and thought of Disraeli’s dictum : “ A man
may resolve to be great, hut only a woman can
make him so.”

At a N.S.W. back-country church after
two inches of rain. A hot-headed, new-chum
priest (after a stormy interview with a freeborn
Australian committee, who refuse to be “sat on”
like those of the old country):—“ You are liars,
renegades and rebels, and I lave you my blessing
in the shape of the dust from off my feet.” Voice
from the committee : “It is mud, you mean,
Father.” His reverence : “And maybe it’s mud
you’ll be wanting in this world, for you won’t
get it in the next.” (Exit.)
Arvie Aspinall’s Alarm Clock.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
In one of these years a paragraph appeared in a
daily paper to the effect that a constable had dis-
covered a little hoy asleep on the steps of Grinder
Bros.’ big factory at 4 o’clock one rainy morning.
The constable woke the hoy and demanded an
explanation.
The little fellow explained that ho ‘ worked
there and was “frightened of being late”; he
“ started wirk at six,” and was apparently greatly
astonished to hear that it was only four. Ihe
constable examined a small paper parcel which
the poor, frightened child carried in his hand.
The parcel contained a clean apron and three
slices of “ bread-and-treacle.”
The child further explained that he woke up
and thought it was late, and didn’t like to wake
“mother” and ask her the time “ because she’d
been washin’.” He didn’t look at the clock,
because they “ didn’t have none.” He volunteered
no explanation as to how he expected “mother”
to know the time, hut, perhaps, like many other
mites of his kind, he had unbounded faith in the
infinitude of a mother’s wisdom. His name was
Arvie Aspinall, please sir, and he lived in Jones’
Alley. Father was dead.
A few days later the same paper took great
pleasure in stating-in reference to that “Touch-
ing Incident” —noticed in a recent issue—that a
benevolent society lady had started a subscription
among her friends with the object of purchasing
an alarm clock for the little hoy found asleep at
Grinder Bros.’ workshop door.
Later on, it was mentioned, “in connection with
the touching incident, that the alarm-clock had
been bought and delivered to the hoy’s mother,
who appeared to be quite overcome with gratitude.
We learn, also, from another sourc". that the
last assertion was greatly exaggerated j|
The touching incident was worn out in another
paragraph, which left no doubt that the benevo-
lent society lady was none other than a charming
and accomplished daughter of the house of
Grinder.
11.
It was late in the last day of the Easter holi-
days, during which Arvie Aspinall lay in bed with
a bad cold. He was still what he called “croopy.”
It was about mine o’clock, and the business of
Jones’ Alley was in full swing.
“That’s better, mother, I’m better far,” said
Arvie, “ the sugar and vinegar cuts the phlegm,
and the hothrin’ cough gits out.” It got out to
such an extent for the next few minutes that he
couldn’t speak. When he recovered his breath,
he said ;
“Better or worse, I’ll haveter go to work to-
morrow, in any case. Gimme the clock, mother.”
“ I tell you, you shall not go ! It will be your
death.”
“ It’s no use talking, mother ; we can’t starve—
and—sposin’ somebody got my place ! Gimme
the clock, mother.”
“I’ll send one of the children round to say
you’re ill. They’ll surely let you off for a day ox-
two. ”
“ ’Taint no use ; they won’t wait; I know them
—what does Grinder Bros, care if I'm ill? Never
mind, mother, I’ll rise above ’em all yet. Give
me the clock, mother.”
She gave him the clock, and he proceeded to
wind it up, and set the alarm.
“There’s somethin’ wrong with the gong,” he
muttered, “ it’s gone wrong two nights now, but
I’ll chance it. I’ll set the alarm at five, that’ll
give me time to dress and git there early. I wish
I hadn’t to walk so far.”
He paused to read some words engraved round
the dial
Early to bed and early to rise
Makes a man healthy and wealthy and wise.
He had read the verse often before, and was
much taken with the swing and rhyme of it. He
had ofun repeated it to himself, over and over
again, without reference to the sense or philosophy
of it. He had never diearned of doubting any-
thing in print, and this was engraved. But now
a new light seemed to dawn upon him. He
studied the sentence awhile, and then read it
aloud for the second time. He turned it over in
his mind again in silence. Then a bright idea
seemed to strike him.
“ Mother ! ’ lie said suddenly, “ I think it lies.”
She placed the clock on the shelf, tucked him into
his little bed on the sofa, and blew out the light.
111.
Arvie seemed to sleep, hut she lay awake think-
ing of her troubles. Of her husband carried home
dead from his work one morning; of her eldest
son who only came to loaf on her when he was out
of gaol; of the second son, who had feathered his
nest in another city, and had no use for her any
longer ; of the next —poor, delicate little Arvie—
struggling manfully to help, and wearing his
young life out at Grinder Bros, when he should he
at schcol; of the five helpless younger children
asleep in the next “room;” of her hard life—
scrubbing offices from 5.30 a.m. till 8, and then
starting her day’s work—washing ! —of having to
rear her children in the atmosphere of brothels,
because she could not afford to shift and pay a
higher rent; and of the rent.
Ai vie commenced to mutter in his sleep.
“Can't you get to sleep, Arvie?” she asked.
“Is your throat sore? Can I get anything for
you ? ”
“I'd like to sleep,” he muttered, dreamily,
“hut it won't seem mor'n a moment before —•
before ”
“Before what, Arvie?’ she asked, quickly,
fearing Unit he was becoming delirious.
“ Before the alarm goes off !”
He was talking in his sleep.
She rose gently and put the alarm on two hours,
“ lie can rest now,” she whispered to herself.
Presently Arvie sat bolt upright, and said
quickly : “ Mother ! I thought the alarm went
off !” Then, without waiting for an answer, he
lay down as suddenly and slept.
* # #
The rain had cleared away, and a bright,
starry dome was over sea and city, over slum and
cilia alike ; but little of it could he seen from the
hovel in Jones’ Alley, save a glimpse of the
Southern Cross and a few stars round it. It
was what ladies call a “lovely night,” as seen
from the house of Grinder— “Grinderville” —with
its moonlit terraces and gardens sloping gently
to the water, and its windows lit up for an Easter
ball, and its reception-rooms thronged by its own
exclusive set, and one of its charming and accom-
plished daughters melting a select party to tears
with her pathetic recitation.
There was something wrong with the alarm-
clock, or else Mrs. Aspinall had made a mistake,
for the gong sounded startlingly in the dead of
night. She woke with a great start, and lay still,
expecting to hear Arvie get up; but he made no
sign. She turned a white, frightened face towards
the sofa where he lay—the light from the alley’s
solitary lamp on the pavement above shone down
thro’ the window, and she saw that he hadn’t
moved.
Why didn’t the clock wake him ? He was
such a light sleeper ! “ Arvie ! ” she called ;no
answer. “Arvie!” she called again, with a
strange ring of remonstrance mingling with the
terror in her voice. Arvie never answered.
“ Oh ! my God ! ” she moaned.
She rose and stood by the sofa. Arvie lay on
his hack with his arms folded—a favourite sleeping
position of his ; but his eyes were wide open and
staring upwards as though they would stare
through ceiling and Yoof to the place where God
,ought to he.
He was dead.
“My God ! My God :! ” she cried.

Of the 498 general officers of the (Con-
federate army, there are .still 174, or 35 per,cent.,

general now living, and he, though 74 years .old,
is remarkably well preserved.

labourers.
A pensioned N.S.W. railway station-
master, whose last billet was on tbe Blue Moun-
tains, in conversation the other day, ridiculed the
idea that locomotive-drivers never make up time.
“ Why,” he said, “I have, in handing them the
staff, frequently drawn attention to the fact that
they were ‘behind time.’” “Oh,” the reply
would be, “ we’ll make that up ” —and they did.

We beceivocl a ietlbr fl'om an Englishman
Hibbthet daV. The first line contained an allusion
!bo “my agent in London,” and in tlie next six
lines he spoke of “my solicitor,” “my banker,”
“my hotel,” “my house ” and “my wife.” Then
he finished up with another allusion to “my
agent” and “my hotel,” and in the postscript ha
mentioned “my wife ” over again. If an English-
man occupies a six-storey room for two nights in a
caravanserai where 80b other people hang oilt, he
alludes to that place as “my hotel ” for the Pest of
his life, and if somebody dra\Vs o'rt him at sight
through a little branch bank, ahd lie doesn’t pay,
be calls tlie place “ my banker ” for ever after-
wards. Yet he doesn’t own the bank, or the
hotel, or the solicitor —and very often he doesn’t
own the wife either. He has only got an interest
in them—about as much interest in one as in the
other.

Squatter Buliant came to town, a woman
talked to him late at night at a street corner, and
he missed a <£so note out of his vest pocket,
whereupon he grabbed her, and handed her over
to the police. The female searcher, at the watch-
house, found naught, wherefore the woman was
sent away. A fortnight afterwards, in tears, she
met the arresting constable, and told him she had
stolen the note, but wished to make restitution.
He concurred witli her proposal, that, while he
looked the other way, she should slip it under the
cuff of his great coat. He would honorably
promise not to take it out till he got to the watch-
house. This would get him the glory of having
recovered the note, and he would attain his
ambition, which was his “ promotion to the plain
clothes.” The transaction was duly accomplished,
but on reaching the watchhouse, he could not find
any note. The woman had slipped it under his
cuff on the first occasion, which accounted for the
non-success of the female searcher, and had now
taken it out.

Dear Bulletin, —He that alleged romantic
finding of a will in an old book the other day,
here's A true stilly. There was a cattle dealer iii
Northern Victoria, who had A youth in his
employment whom he always said he would make
his heir. The old man was pretty “well in,” so
it’s not surprising that when he died; arid no. will
could be ftiuild, a next-of-kip—sixteenth Cpuaihoir
so—turfled Up from South Australia and took the
lot. The young fellow —Brown will do for his
name—went down to Melbourne, got pretty hard-
up, and had to make many visits to the pawnshop.
Amongst other things he pawned a gun, and
thought no more of the matter until one day, as
he was passing the pawnshop, the broker rushed
out after him. “ You’re the chap that left a gun
with me about a month back, ain’t you?” “Yes.”
“ Well, come inside; I’ve got something-to slio'v
■Vdi}.*! The something was the missing will, which
had been rolled up tight and shoved down the
barrel of the gun. It left Brown .£4OOO, and,
when it had been duly proved, the pawnbroker
was made .£5O richer for his little find. —A.M.

liament contributed three guineas to a School of
Arts in his constituency, whereupon the secretary
wrote back like this :—“Dear Sir, —Many thanks
for your liberal subscription. The committee have
another favour to ask of you ; they intend having
scientific lectures delivered in the institute, and,
for the purpose qf demonstration, require a humnij
skeleton—an articulated one, if possible. It would
be well if some interesting history attached to the
skeleton. Can you oblige us in this direction ?”

to the sexton of the local cemetery, who, he
thought, might be able to help ’em.

THE WISHING-BONE.
With fingers link’d to the osseous wrong,
And light, love-laden eye-gleams eyeward speeding,
In laughing rivalry they wrested long,
As loth the fracture ; then the joyous sohg
Of her rich mirth died in this wistful pleading;
“ What vvas’t you wish’d? Ah, love! ” Soft roS? her sighs-
“ ’Tis thirteen years to-day since we were married.”
She lean’d towards him, and her warm blood dyes
Glowed in her cheeks, and love swam in her eyes,
“ Tell me my husband—have you never wearied ? ”
Then gazing in the wells of molten blue,
The witchy radiance of her presence shedding
A glow all through his soul, he fondly drew
Her to him, saying, with the love she knew—
“ I wish’d, my darling—for a golden wedding.”
Ballarat, June 1.

It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,”
80 and 82 EHzabeth-street, near King-street, is
just the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

The same correspondent:—
Is not the Australian press mistaken in insinuating
that Bertrand’s detention in gaol, after so many years’
imprisonment, is due to the fact that certain entries in
his diary affect the good name of “ a certain lady con-
nected with a powerful official ?” I am aware that this
belief is current, but 1 can safely deny it. I had means of
knowing a great deal about the case at the time it was
tried, and had access to all the documents which were
taken possession of by the police, and I do not recollect
any such entry in a diary. 1 have no doubt that the story
emanates from Bertrand himself, who is a remarkably
cunning, clever fellow, but intensely vain, and prone to
boast of imaginary conquests amongst ladies in good
society. I knew a good deal of the man when he prac-
tised as a dentist, and I think I should have known of any
■such bonne fortune. Depend upon it that the story you
quote is untrue, and Bertrand is kept in gaol because he
committed a very wanton murder, his victim being his
supposed friend. If no good can result from keeping him
in confinement, what but harm could accrue from his
liberation ? He would undoubtedly have been hanged but
for the astuteness of lus counsel, who took an Untenable
point of law. After keeping the man so long with his
neck in jeopardy, it was decided to take a merciful view
of his case, and the death penalty was commuted.
Gov. Kintore has greatly hurt Adelaide
society by omitting to give a Birthday ball.
Broken Hill shares are not high enough to allow
of extravagance, and what is £5OOO a year vice-
regal salary, when sons have to be kept at Eton
and daughters are looking out for marriage por-
tions ?
* # *
* * *
Some of the doctors of Adelaide write their
prescriptions in a kind of cipher which only one
chemist can understand, and they collect quite
valuable royalties every year from the proceeds of
this pious fraud on their patients.
* * »
The liquidation of the Commercial Bank of
S. A., which has dragged on for six years and cost
about £40,000 in law-expenses, was again before
the Adelaide Supreme Court last week. It was
touching to look at six heavily-feed counsel sym-
pathetically listening to an even more heavily-paid
Q. G. pleading that the thing should be settled
and no more law-costs incurred. Finally, the
Court ordered a special meeting to be held to ask
the shareholders whether they have had enough
law, or whether they would like to “blew’’the
£20,000 in hand in an action against the directors?
Last week a humiliated husband appeared
in Melbourne detective-office. A woman had
robbed him of a magnificent watch, with the
inscription, “Presented by his affectionate wife.”

When you seethe advt. “Housekeeper,
experienced, with child, wishes situation in

child is the result of the experience.
# # *
Percival Dawe. committed suicide at Flem-
mgton (Melb.) the other day. “He led an austere
life,” says the Standard, “and was attached to
the Flemington brass band.”
# * #
In Melbourne District Court. “ What
will become of you in the next world if you don't
tell the truth ?” asked the magistrate of a small
boy. “ Dunno what'll happen t’ me in this world,
let alone the next,” gloomily responded the boy.
A Melbourne Customs’ official, earning
£350 a year, suicided the other day, whereupon
several hundred applicants for the post turned up.
The department, to stop the rush, announced in
the papers that the vacancy would not be filled at
least for some time.
9 * *
The other day a Sydney quack doctor,
accused of being mixed up in the death of the
inevitable young woman from the inevitable cause,
explained that he had no fixed scale of charges,
but judged people from their appearance. If a
man came to him wearing a heavy gold chain, for
instance, he would not charge him less than half-a-
guinea. It is often dangerous to wear a gold
chain. At “The Statue,” in Sydney, the other
evening, someone was taking up a collection for
the unemployed, “ Only 12 bob more wanted to
give each man a feed,” he said. “Put your fat
gold chain into the ’at,” kindly remarked a
bystander,
* * #
They are finding the corpses of “infants
unknown ” in all sorts of places about Melbourne,
and in great numbers, but it is remarkable that in
all cases they are discovered either by women or
children. The average man, when he comes across
a suspicious parcel, remembers his wife’s cold,
cruel eye and the sarcasms of the bar-room cynics,
and glides by on the other side. No wise man
ever found a baby alive or dead.
* # *
A cheerfully - morbid Manly resident
bought a coffin for himself a few years ago. Mean-
while he keeps whisky in it.
The last “high-
toned ” surprise-party in
certain Australian city
came to a bad end. There
were eighteen of them,
nine being ladies in thin
boots, and they went out
to a remote suburb in
nine cabs, carrying the
o rthodox refreshments
with them. To avoid
drawing attention they
dismissed the cabs round
the corner, and walked
the rest of the way. It
began to rain just as they
arrived, and while they
were waiting for admit-
tance it started to come
down in torrents. The
door was opened by a
flunkey who said the
family was “hout.” They
remarked that they would
come in and wait, but
the menial looked with a
suspicious eye at the
packages and hampers,
and said they had better
call again. All this time
the last eight people at
the. back of the crowd
were getting damp. Then
they explained that they
were a surprise - party,
but the stolid man of
calves merely responded
in a wooden fashion that
he was sorry but the
family was ‘‘ hout. ”
Finally he shut the door
in their faces, and when
they still remained on
the verandah he threat-
ened to call for the
police. Then they went.
They travelled two miles
through bogs and slush
ere they found a cab-
stand, and by that time
the ladies were soaked
to the skin and half
frozen and nearly dead.
Most of them arrived
home in tears, and had
to put their feet in hot
water, and it is ques-
tionable if any of them
will ever be quite the
same female again.

They have a girl in the Melbourne Post-
office who can lick and stick 3000 envelopes in an
hour. It has been remarked that she takes the
cake. Nobody else but a cake would let her take
him. The licking and sticking power of the
average woman is enough for the wise man.
The bard has actually crawled into the
Age birth-advts.
A little boy, he counts one more
In this fair land ;
May he be one who will adore
His God’s Right Hand.
When “it” is triplets the bard can easily make
this an obituary for the old man : —
Another batch, they count three more
In this fair land;
lint, scared, their father fled before
To join the feathered band.

If there is anything in the allegation that
a S. Australian clergyman took a disgraceful
advantage of his position as instructor of a girls’
confirmation class, why don’t the parents of the,
girls prosecute him instead of hushing the matter
up “ for the good of the church ?”
# # *
It is suggested that Lieut, Colonel Gordon
(who recently married an attractive wife and
many shekels in Melbourne) shall succeed Major-
General Downes as Commander of the S.A. army.
* # #
The best-matured man in Sydney—he is
also one of the richest—lately changed his address,
and now lives out by the sad sea waves. The
beggars get there all the same. They root him
out of bed in the morning, and are heartily cursed,
but his oaths and his gold always go together.
* * *
It would be politic for Salvation Army
officers to go about a bit seedy, and not always in
such spick-and-span new-looking clothes. It
insults the subscribers, not to speak of the
unemployed.
# # *
Preacher Grubb the other day stormed the
quiet little town of Picton (M.L.), and an awful
frenzy resulted. The congregations were, as
usual, mostly women, and Grubb shrieked at
them, and told them about Sheol, and the last
day, and the horrible note of Gabriel’s horn, and
the rising of the dead, and the rushing of billions
of ghosts through the sunless sky, and the tramp
of milliards of skeletons, and bulgy things with-
out flesh or feet, and slimy devils, and goblins,
and cuttle-fish all walking upon air, and the open-
ing of perdition, and the yells of the lost souls all
rushing for the Gate, till his audience went into
hysterics and the church was full of shrieks.
When the service was over, the terrified congrega-
tion wouldn’t quit, but being turned out at last
they knelt in the muddy street to the utter ruin of
their frocks and stockings, and prayed, and wept
bitterly, and groaned with an exceeding great
groan till near midnight. Then their male rela-
tives began to come for them, and every now and
then a man would find his wife or his sister or his
daughter among the slush, and would carry her
home praying violently as she went. But the
widows, or those whose relatives preferred
to let them slide, stayed on and supplicated, and
gave all their money to Grubb, and confessed their
sins till 12 o’clock, when they mostly made for
home, though the last penitent wasn’t shifted till
12.30. And, as the result of all this fervour, there
is hardly a frock left in the town that isn’t ruined
with mud, and nearly every woman has told all
her principal sins to all the other women in the
place, and done considerable damage to her
character thereby. Women always get ‘ ‘ left ” thus
in a revival. The fashionable bank embezzler
never gives way to pious emotion for fear of dis-
arranging his tie ; but when a hundred women are
gathered together, with Grubb in the chair, they
presently let down their back hair, and tear
off their bonnets, and give themselves away
till their dresses all rip down the back. There is
no cure for this either —except the sudden death
of Pastor Grubb.
* * *
Apropos of Hebrew loyalty. It was Zox
who procured A. T. Clark, M.P., the flogging from
Zulu Moodie, of Adelaide. Zox brought under
the notice of Parliament the article in Clark’s
paper, the Williamstown Advertiser, referring
to Her Gracious as “an obese personage.” Ker-
fercl, as a Minister, was anxious to go on with a
Local Government Bill, and pooh-poohed the in-
troduction of the personage, but Zox was not to
bo done out of his loyalty. “ Mr. Speakah ! Am
I to stand calmly by and see Her Gracious
Majesty insulted ?” Kerferd caved in with a
groan, and Zox spouted on for about 20 minutes,
well knowing that every word would be reported.

Gov. Jersey laid the foundation-stone of
the Newcastle (N.S.W.) Anglican Cathedral one
day last week. In 1826 Capt. Wallis, the local
commandant, laid the foundation “ rubble ” of the
old one. Military officers In “ them days ” wera
pious folk.

down to half-size. So you see, even We suffer.

“ There are plenty of happy marriages,
but no one ever hears about them." says Cecil
Norton, in Jerome’s “Barbara.” True, all the
way. Marriage is chiefly a failure in the law-
court columns of the evening fifth edition.

Last year the population ot Brisbane
decreased by about 13,000 people, and there were
nearly 4000 houses to let in the city and suburbs
on 31st March last.

Age, May 25 :— *
Some time before the Queen’s Birthday vice regal
reception was to commence the St. Kilda-road was
thronged by cabs and pedestrians all clad in fitting attire
for the function, but ridiculously inappropriate for every-
thing else.
Just sit down and think hard.

A doubly-divorced Sydney man is well
under way with his third wife.
* * *
The legal and medical professions in Bris-
bane are torn by internecine feuds. The former
is wise enough to keep its trouble to itself; the
latter isn’t,
# ‘ # #
The tall, silent feature of the new Gaiety
company is understood to be Miss Foote, not Feet
—as might be expected. Rumours state that a cer-
tain Judge may shortly find himself the father-
in-law of this colossal vision.
* * *
Asa burly policeman up North was
spreading out his feet to catch the evening breeze,
the other day, a man rushed up and informed him
that there was a dead body in the river. He at
once rose to the importance of his position.
Giving half-a-dozen orders as to a van, a coffin,
the medical officer, &0., he hurried to the river
bank, and there, sure enough, two boys in a punt
had towed the gruesome object to the shore. It
was a dead calf.
# * »
Queensland is suffering from economy-in-
patches. For years the clothes of the telegraphic
messengers were made by one firm, which gave
ample satisfaction as to fit and material. This
year the contract has been given to a “cheaper ”
man, the reduction being nearly 30s. per suit.
The visible result can be imagined. The unfor-
tunate boys look either like scarecrows or “bell-
bottomed ” larrikins. But the Government are
satisfied, and the saving will help to pay the
.£12,000 voted for the Auxiliary Squadron.
* * *
When people say, ‘ 1 What a nice little
boy !” they mean what nice clothes he’s got.
* * *
Isn’t it a proof that hospitals and maternity
institutions are only places where the poor are
experimented on, for the benefit of the rich, when
you see how little the rich care to experiment on
starving people ?
9 * *
The Melbourne journeymen butchers had
an extraordinary gala last week ; thousands of
spectators turned out to gaze on a mere hour-long
procession of butcher’s carts. What was the
fascination ? The spectacle of a host of jolly-
looking fellows, able to impose tolerably fair terms
on their employers, while depression reigns all
round. Meanwhile, there is the rush after miser-
able clerks’ billets. Respectability !
# # #
The Caledonian Financial Association has
its grip on Melbourne Hibernian Hall, for £20,000
—and they talk of foreclosing ! When Shamus
Torlough Shanaghan O’Brallaghan went over
from Carrickfergus and peopled all Scotland with
his own hands (so he did), he never thought of how
his descendant, Tonalt of the Mist, would get his
divvle’s paws on that hall, and threaten to turn
the Eirish out.
* * •
Percy Da we, a fervent Wesleyan and
member of the Kensington brass band, shot him-
self through the head last week. The misguided
young man had a theory that all music was the
actual voice of God, and he was wdnt to blow his
clarionet with an air of rapt attention, as though
he wanted to find out what heaven was saying to
the unfortunate audience.
* * *
A notable coincidence is that the young
man to whom Miss Rounsevell was to have been
married when in Queensland, is himself an impor-
tant witness in a murder-trial at next Brisbane
Supreme Court sittings.
* * *
Anent the clerical drawl : There is a
“divine (what is the origin of this misused term ?)
ministering not a hundred miles from Parramatta
whose final “ ah ” or “ er ” is sublimely ridiculous.
Imagine, if you can, the distortion he inflicts on
the following sentences phonetically understood :
“ Gift up your hearts and live—er ; ” “One foot
in the grave, and the other all but—er.” —W.G.C.
TO REST.
Across the borderland of day,
Through fields of sunflecked foam,
By frowning hills of sombre grey,
The red sun wanders home.
And mellow grows the changeful West
In misty curtains furled ;
And mystic voices sing “To rest,
To rest, O weary world.”
“ To rest, to rest,” they softly sing,
“ To rest, O weary sea;
To rest, to rest, O tired wing—
But what, O Night, of me ? ”
Brisbane, June, 1892.
K.
IMPORTANT CABLEGRAM.
“Sir William Clarke, Bart., was present
at the re-election of H.R.II. the Prince of Wales
as Grand Master of the English Freemasons.”
How pleased the Prince of Wales must be
(And proud his Royal Highness, too !)
When some great Austral absentee
Looms suddenly into his view !
The newspapers, too, grow impressed
And heedless of the inky glare ;
In massive type the fact attest—
“ Sir William Clarke, the millio aii c !”
Oh ! potent spell which hedges round
The moneyed man with world-wide fame,
There seems a sort of bullion sound
Even in the jingle of his name !
It matters not if great or good,
It matters not from whence or where
His presence awes the multitude—
“ Sir William Clarke, the millionaire 1 ”
Let humble merit die unknown,
Let beauly sink in vice and shame ;
Can aught for poverty atone,
Youth, innocence, a spotless name.
The cold, hard world stands sternly by
Or grimly mocks the mute despair,
While hark ! a million voices cry
“ Sir William Clarke, the millionaire 1”
Victorian Masons learn with glee
Their figurehead lias met the Prince,
And know to such a “ chief ” as he
His Royal Highness will evince
The very cream of fellowship—
Enough to make the whole world stare—
For is he not—hurrah ! hip ! hip ! —
“ Sir William Clarke, the millionaire !”
Then flash like some bejewelled thing
The sparkling message through the soa—
More swift than bird upon the wing,
More bright than gem of Arahy ! —
But well may Science fume and fret,
Her proudest feat, forsooth, must hear
Tbe news—that H.R.H. has met,
“ Sir William Clarke, the millionaire !”
E.M.M.

A cottage home for male inebriates is to
be established at Middle Harbour (Sydney) as
soon as the neoessary additional funds are
obtained. Subscriptions sent to the secretary,
Trafalgar House, 0 Princes-street, Sydney, will be

It is related that when the doughty Con-
federate, Captain Semmes, practised law in
Mobile after the war he was one day arguing a
ease in court before his son, who was .) iidge. The
Judge ruled him out, and the old man made some
nasty remarks as to las son’s legal knowledge.
After a sharp tilt of words between father and
son, the Judge said: “At home, sir, 1 respect
you—but I want you to understand that this
court has no more respect for you than the
humblest citizen in the country. Take your seat,
sir !” This story wouldn’t fit in Australia, where
the old man is always the Judge and doesn’t
respect his son a bit.
A certain Australian agnostic lecturer and
Ids wife, some time ago, deserted the freethought
platform for the orthodox rostrum. Before their
‘ conversion,” it is related, the orator and his wife
were staying at a provincial town. Likewise
touring there was a dramatic Cos. One Sunday-, as
w ,8 usual, the gentle agnostic and Madame went
o it to rake in the cash of the unbeliever, leaving
their infant to sleep the sleep of the innocent- or
howl, as it might happen ; as things turned out it
howled and disturbed the rest of two mummers in
the adjoining room. They were both devout
Catholics, and considering the infant to be on the
point of death, were troubled about its soul, so they
got up and baptised it according to the method
prescribed in such cases by the Holy Roman ritual
—in cases where laymen have reasonable grounds
for suspecting that an infant is in danger of dying
before a duly-ordained priest can be summoned.
In the hard old times of unbelief Madame’s most
successful hash-raiser used to be : “Why I left the
Roman Catholic Church ! ” How the infant
secretly returned to that church is possibly a secret
to its parents unto this very day.
Funny
IRISH
BLUNDERS
THE native of the Green Isle who said, “It’s
a great comfort to be alone when your
sweetheart is with you,” and the Irish auctioned
who, while praising some goods he was offering
for sale, exclaimed, “These are just the things I
should like to buy for my wife if she were a
widow,” made very witty bulls. Equally good,
but even more complicated, was that perpetrated
by the exile in America, who pathetically ex-
pressed himself thus: “Sure an’ if I live till I
die, an’God knows whether I will or not, I’ll visit
ould Oireland afore I lave Philadelfy.” A funnier
blunder still was that made by a new-chum
Hibernian, who was a passenger by the Southern
express the other night. He alighted, with other
thirsty souls, at Mittagong, but lingered at tha
bar over hisfavourite nectar longer than he should,
and only regained the platform in time to see tha
tail-lights of the train by which he intended to
have journeyed, receding from his view. For a
moment he was dumb with astonishment, and
then collecting all the energy and presence of
mind he was capable of exerting, he cried,
“ Hould on there, ye blatherin’ engine; falx'
there’s a man on board of ye that’s left behind !”
1 hese stories have all the true Irish ring about
• them, and call up memories of the Irishman who
complained to his physician that he “gave him so
many drugs he was sick a long time after lie got
well.” No one who has used St. Jacobs Oil can
make a similar complaint to that; it never causes
a man to “feel sick after he is well.” St. Jacobs
Oil is an external remedy for the absolute cure of
pain, and it leaves no ill effects behind. After
using it the rheumatic patient leaves his bed, or
throws away his crutches and begins life again a
new man. Hear what Mr. F. W. Kirby, of Mort-
street, Balmain, says about it: “I use St. Jacobs
Oil for rheumatism, and do not know what i
should do without it. The kind of work I am
engaged in compels me to be out in all weathers,
and I often have to remain in wet clothes for
hours together. The result it that I get rheu-
matism badly in my back at times, I find there
is nothing equal to St. Jacobs Oil for shifting it,
and putting me all right again. Both mv wife
and I look upon it as a real blessing. The volun-
tary testimony of thousands of such witnesses
proves that this marvellous remedy possesses the
confidence of all c’asses of the community.
THEY GO UNDER WATER.
A SUPREME TEST.
A brave man is always a truthful man. His
•whole nature is opposed to anything that savors of
sham or deceit, and he will gladly welcome death
in the discharge of a duty rather than face dis-
honor. It is a truism that
Cowards die many times. . .
The valiant never taste of death but once.
Pessimists may croak that there is always the
man for the occasion, provided his services are
well paid for. Well, that’s true enough in a way,
but the easy remunerative billet is always the one
that draws out the greatest number of applicants.
It is wonderful how many men, having an eye to
the main chance, and recognising the value of
<£3oo a year, fancy themselves fit persons to
represent a constituency in the Legislature. But
then the hazard there is small, and lias a reference
only to the time when power is in the hands of
the electors. Amongst men of pluck and daring
are those who “go down to the sea in ships,’ 7
and the still more adventurous persons whose
calling takes them down beneath the turbulent
green waves of the ocean, or the swift running
current of the treacherous river, that they
may recover treasures not their own, or place
foundations destined to produce wealth for
others. Men like these who possess merit them-
selves are not slow to recognise it in others, hence
the following letter addressed to the local agent of
the Waterbury Watch Company, by Mr. T. .1.
Fawkner, submarine diver, Harbors and Rivers
Department, Maclean, Clarence River, under date
18th January, 1892 : “Waterbury Watch received
safely. I have worn it for over three years, and
only had to send it for repairs through an accident
(falling out of my hand). I must say that I never
had such an excellent timekeeper, t often take it
under water with me, and it goes as well under-
water as on top, and is as good now as the day I
got it.” These watches are now obtainable in a
variety of patterns and finish, but all equally good
and reliable.

KARANA.
WHEN the digestive organs-are out of order
a good reliable tonic is required to bring
them back to their normal state.
A number oi persons upset the stomach by
neglecting to take their meals at regular stated
intervals, and others again cat to excess at some
particular meal —and from these causes the
machinery gets out of order and does not run
smoothly. Late suppers, also, are one of the
causes ofupsetting the digestive organs.
Sister Mary Joseph Aubert, in her researches
amongst the plants of New Zealand, has dis-
covered there is a very valuable remedy for these
complaints in our own vegetation, and after ex-
perimenting for a number of years, J' as now
brought forward the medicine called KAKAJN A.
Dr. Larochette, a chemist of the First Class
cf the Faculty of Paris, to whom samples were
sent, says “Itis an excellent bitter tonic, and
I consider it even superior to the preparation of
Cinchona in all affections of the stomach. It has
given us, prepared as, you sent it, the most satis-
factory results.” .. V •
The Reverend Sister says she has used it tor
many years extensively and successfully with
sufferers of every age and constitution, and she
noticed that in a few cases it did not agree at first',
but by diminishing the dose good results followed.
She remarked astonishing good results on old
people, who had ruined their constitutions by the
abuse of intoxicating drink, and who were debili-
tated and suffering after having turned over a
new leaf.
ENQUIRE WITHIN!
DO you suffer from Headache and Dizziness in
the head ; have you pains under the ribs or
shoulders ; are you affected with heartburn or in-
digestion ? If so, have you ever asked yourself
why this is? You certainly are allowing two of
the chief organs in your structure to become
neglected. The liver and kidneys are responsible
for these ill feelings, and you are getting too much
wc ,ik out of them without attending to their
wants in the particular functions they have to
sustain. . ,
These organs are in continual play day and
night, waking or sleeping, and any constitution
will not stand, be it ever so good, if its internal
requirements are not attended to.
The liver and kidneys cannot put up with re-
pudiation, and they soon let you know it, and it
is highly necessary that “Paramo” should be
administered to their wants. This vegetable pre-
paration is particularly adapted to these com-
plaints, and those persons who have already taken
it assure us they never felt better in their lives.
Mother Mary Joseph Aubert has been particu-
larly successful with “ Paramo,” and is daily
receiving numberless enquiries in reference to it.
The agents are continually getting letters and
telegrams from all parts of New Zealand, and
have confidence in saying that no other remedies
are giving the same amount of general satisfaction.
Give “Paramo ” a fair trial, and note how
quickly your mental depression leaves you. It is
worth the experiment, and will certainly do no

Nkw Zealand Tablet, 16th October, 1891.
The religious orders continue to maintain their
reputations as benefactors of. Society in temporal
as well as in spiritual matters. A new proof of
this is furnished in the remedies which have been
discovered by the Sister Mary Joseph Aubert.
The good Sister, whose-life has been devoted to
the instruction of the Maoris, while she has
laboured successfully at improving their minds
and enlightening their spirits, has not been
neglectful of their bodies. The result is the dis-
covery of a series of medicines manufactured from
native trees and plants, and which have been
proved undeniably to be of almost marvellous
efficacy. It is to the credit of the Catholic Church
in the Colony that one of its missionary Sisters
has distinguished herself in this way, probably
conferring on the world a benefit, little, if any-
thing, inferior to that conferred, for example, by
the Jesuit Fathers who discovered the curative
properties of the Peruvian bark. Not only suffer-
iiw humanity but science itself owes a debt to
this lady, who has made it acquainted with pro
parties of natural products, hitherto unsuspected,
and has enriched the pharmacopoeia. We have,
further, in the matter, an instance of how
relig ous devotion is calculated to quicken the
faculties and to develop the best qualities of
human nature. Sister Mary Joseph is to be
warmly commended for her discoveries, and in-
deed, the community generally may be congratu-
lated on them.

An old eccentric identity, Dominie
Dressier, for 53 years a resident of Bathurst,
N.S.W., died last week, aged 80. The old man’s
“Tales of the System” were astounding.

I and My Breams.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Through long, dim years we have been friends together,
I and my dreams ;
Our friendship has been tried by wintry weather,
I and my dreams.
That oft we’ve quarrelled freely I will own,
And travelled each a different way alone,
I and my dreams.
We could not always make each other out,
I and my dreams ;
My dreams were bright—my life a stormy bout.
I and my dreams
Unclasped our hands, I fell to wond’ring whether
We would he friends, or friendship's ties should sever,
I and my dreams.
We watched the sun set in his western bed,
1 and my dreams ;
We marked the golden path his bright rays shed,
I and my dreams.
And as we marked the glory rise and swell, each sought
To read aright, the others’ pensive thought,
I and my dreams.
And then we smiled a sadly wistful smile,
I and my dreams,
And thought we’d keep together for life’s little while,
I and my dreams ;
It may be in that western world of light
We’ll walk, with hands still clasped, from this world’s
night,
I and my dreams.
Sofala (N.S.W.).
L. WYATT.
The one juryman who hung out in favour
of respondent Michel in the big Sydney divorce-
case, and so rendered the twelve days’ proceedings
utterly abortive, declared that he did not believe
the evidence produced in support of the charge of
adultery made against Michel.
A new Australian magazine, to be called
The Antipodean, will, in November next, be
published simultaneously in London and Australia.
It will be jointly edited by Mr. J. T. Ryan, a
promising young pressman, and Mr. G. Essex-
Evans, who will endeavour to fly high from the
start.
Dan. Mayne has leased his paper, the
Bathurst Sentinel, to a syndicate of comps.
He returns to live in his native village, Windsor
(N.S.W.), and will continue on the turf. Dan.’s
original trade was slipper-making; his father’s
that of a “nailer,” the old-fashioned way of mak-
ing nails, of course.
An American, who lately visited Berlin,
writes that he saw a magnificent review by the
Emperor. Then he went a little out of town and
saw a woman holding the plough, while two other
women and a dog pulled it. We daresay. In the
Wimmera country we have seen the good frau
ploughing in Wellington boots, while the noble
Hans squatted on the boundary-fence with a pipe
in his jowl, and talked to his friend Dinkelspiel.

Army, 101 Queen Victoria-street, London, E.C!.,

also the ‘Merchant.’ These official compilations are
not edited by sentimental enthusiasts evidently,”
remarks an exchange.
A run of ill-luck still follows the magnetists
and faith-healers and the herbal crowd all over
Australia. The last to get into trouble is “pro-
fessor” Burgess of Warrnambool (Vic.), whose
last cancer patient—a girl named Catherine
Brittain —died of blood-poisoning and exhaustion.
The medical witnesses, who always come down like
tons of bricks on the “ professor ” when they can
get at him, declared that the treatment had
accelerated the patient’s death, which was practi-
cally a charge of manslaughter. The jury followed
suit. The coroner denounced the “magnetist”
individual as an “ignorant quack and impostor,’’
and Mr. Burgess is now awaiting trial. And the
moral of all this is that when the bogus “pro-
fessor ” loses a patient he ought to gather up his
properties and run for his life.
Dear Bulletin, —You ask for information
re George Spencer Briggs, Australian litterateur,
now being starred in England. I will tell you
the little I know of a Spencer Briggs—respect-
ing George, I am not sure. Some 15 or 16
years ago there arrived in Armidale a chemist
named Briggs, with his family. They were new
arrivals from Bath, England, and the eldest son,
a very intelligent lad of 14 -or 15, used to assist his
father in a series of lectures on chemistry, which
he gave in Armidale shortly after starting busi-
ness there. The lad’s name was Spencer Briggs.
After a vain endeavour to establish himself in
Armidale, Briggs, sen., moved on to Glen Innes;
anyhow, I never heard of them after they left
Armidale. If my man is identical with G.S.B.,
the Australian litterateur, he is not an Austra-
lian by birth, although, unlike our friend, L. F.
Heydon, he may be one from inclination.—Yours,
Frank Lamb. Chiswick, May 27, 1802.
Mr. Shegog, Governor of Melbourne Gaol,
supplied Deeming with his last earthly smoke. The
wretch ran out of cigars (he couldn’t take to a pipe)
on the Sunday night, so the Governor satisfied the
cravings of his charge until his heart was right up
in his mouth and he wanted to smoke no more. The
cell where he had been accommodated until nine
o’clock smelt strongly of cigars when The Bul-
letin’s representative looked in at about
10.30a.m., and whilst the body of the smoker was
hanging from a beam a dozen yards away.
Deeming managed to get through a couple of the
Shegogian weeds after being awakened at seven
o’clock-—the stories of his collapse notwithstand-
in -. As a matter of fact there was no real
co lapse until he had to shift into the condemned
cell, and then he didn't collapse much, all things
considered. For three weeks he remained in his
dungeon smoking like a chimney and refusing to
take exercise. And still he had some nerve left
at the finish. Stick a newspaper reporter (ironed)
on an old horsehair-bottomed chair in a cigar-
scented dungeon for a like period, then pull him
out and ask him to take part in a billiard-match
before a room-full of people. He would look a
lot worse than Denning on the drop, if you
could see hi.o ‘ on the spot ” with the score at
r .. 11 » ur>.
