Dear Bulletin, —I notice in the Govern -
ment Gazette an offer of .£100 reward for the
conviction of the person who lately killed an
Afghan’s camel at Bourke. They only offer <£50
for the discovery of a child-murderer. Is an
Afghan’s camel twice as valuable as a white man’s
child?—The Dingo.
For Dyspepsia and General Depression—Take
Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Madame Melba (Ellen Mitchell) is the
holder of a 1280-acre selection near Mackay (Q.).
The documents were unearthed in Brisbane Lands
Office a week or two ago in connection with pro-
ceedings which her solicitors are taking to have
the land vested in her own name (under the pro-
visions of the recent “Married Woman’s Property
Act ”) apart from her husband to whom it had
been transmitted.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
“ Demos ” writes ; “I am cut to the bone
by the sights of poverty in Melbourne. In South
Melbourne this week I saw a stalwart, sober, in-
dustrious young labourer with his wife and four
children, all preparing to camp out, in this winter
time, when a subscription (by the poor) relieved
them. As a great favour, a poor and honest
woman was allowed to sleep in a cell at the South
Melbourne watchhouse —a place infinitely more
cosy than many dwellings I visit in the neighbour-
hood. From things like this, I glance over to
Government House, beyond on the hill, with the
ball-room windows lighted up, like so many eyes,
in insulting mockery, while the members of Par-
liament are junketing.”
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.

colds and sore throats. New American Bar

A Bend in a Tree.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Standing at the head of a wild mountain gulch,
in a western district, where a slip had cleared the
view, I surveyed the scenery thrown down by
Nature’s hand. Afar, the silvery Pacific lay in
seemingly placid slumber. The mighty spurs
reached down toward the sea like vast claws about
to clutch a basin. Hill and valley were clothed
with forest vegetation. The river rolled rapidly
and irregularly down to the ocean. Below me the
trees clung to the crevices of the rock, and it
seemed imnossible for their naked roots to draw
nutriment from those rents. The day was cap-
tivatingly beautiful —one of the days in a life
when a man would wish to live forever.
At my feet, two men were working in a sluicing
claim. The ground was shallow. Their tail-race
was curved in-and-out to suit circumstances.
Huge trees towered to the skies, and it was part
of the miner’s profession to undermine these
with the nozzle in such a manner that
their fall did not obstruct his future proceedings.
The water was now playing under a withered
forest giant about 80 feet high, with a decided bend
in its trunk some CO feet from the ground. A
small rainbow was visible in the spray under the
jet. I watched with a vague fascination the pro-
gress of their labours.
Presently some dirt fell. Suddenly the tree
swayed and thundered down, seeming to slide at
the roots before it lost its perpendicular. With a
wild yell the men fled in different directions. One
scudded down the tail-race. He reached a bend
in the race and turned at full speed. At that
moment the tree struck the ground. Strange to
say, the bend in its stem exactly fitted the curve in
the race, the weight of the fall and the loss of resist-
ance snapped the piece off, and the broken end
falling into the trench, which was 20 feet deep,
struck the unfortunate miner. From my point of
observation, it seemed as if the tree had been
deliberately cut by an invisible hand.
I hastened to the spot, mentally certain that
Providence had seized a moment when trouble
would follow the accident. I was right. The
bread-winner of a family of eight was gone by this
peculiar arrangement of things. The old tree had
been waiting for a century to get him, and it sue-
ceeded at last.

REV. MOTHER MARY JOSEPH AUBERT.
The Rev. Sister has been for over 30 year B
experimenting, and during that time has succesb-
fully treated hundreds of cases without fee or
reward; and, seeing the great benefit that may
accrue to mankind generally, has consented to
have the medicines put up in saleable form, and
offered to the public under the names of Paramo,
Natanata, Marupa, and Karana, and which
have bepn registered for protection and to avoid
plausible imitations.
A peculiar experience was met with by the Rev.
Mother Mary Joseph Aubort during a recent visit
to Wellington, Being in the garb of her Order
she was recognised, and people who had been
benefited by her wonderful vegetable medicines
and others who intended to undergo a course of
them were so eager to interview the old lady that
she was simply bewildered. Even in her passage
along the streets persons thronged upon her, and
eventually she had to settle down to spending her
time in a cab whenever she wanted to go out of
doors in the Empire City.
Concentrated sunshine is acknowledged to be
Nature’s great remedy for all the ills that human
flesh is heir to. In no part of the world is the
remedy, in rays pure and serene, more freely
lavished than in New Zealand. The sun-
shine playing on a clear, salubrious atmos-
| phere lias left it-> impression on tbf
I fauna and flora of tlie colony. The extinct
moa, the wondrous coal deposits, proclaim the
natural wealth of New Zealand. Tim muscular
Maori and the splendid forests survive. From
these forests Mother Mary Aubert has compounds d
several important rememes, and we recommend
the announcements elsewhere to the perusal nut
simply of the sick but of those in health. “A
stitch in time saves nine ” applies to the healing art
more than to less important matters. Mother
Mary Aubert’s New Zealand Ketnedirs are pi’e-
ventive as well as curative. When the first
symptoms of sickness appear, tl.eir power, in
cutting short the attack by rousing dormant
organs and functions to activity, is said to be
remarkable. Insidious ailments resemble tbe
burglar, but these remedies promptly applied give
the alarm and make him de amp. There is no
quackery about them, they arc not foreign com-
pounds of which people know nothing and which
may be pernicious, but they are the pure products
of New Zealand sunshine distilled through the
vegetable kingdom. Better than all, their char-
acter has been proved for they have been well
tested, and the best proof of their merits is that
their sale is rapidly increasing.

* * *
How did old John Fairfax feel in Heaven when
fhe S.M. Herald came out with a column leader on such
a beastly affair as the Jackson-Slaviu fight? And then
jook at how enterprising the Herald was over Deeming!
* * #
In what was, all things considered, an able
leader on the Slavin-Jackson fight, the S.M. Herald said
the downfall of the P.R. was brought about by an Act for-
bidding railway companies carrying parties to prize-
fights. This is wrong. For instance, how could a ticket-
clerk or station-master discern whether a crowd were on
scrapping intent ? What the Act did was to deter com-
panies from allowing passengers to alight except at recog-
nised railway stations. It was always customary to stop
the train at a secluded part of the line and then pitch
ropes, stakes, &c. Next to the blackguardism of the pugs
themselves, nothing settled the ring so much as the law
which made a spectator to a prize-fight particeps criminis.
Jackson’s rceent victory sustains the old P.R.
theory that a good, straight left can always overcome a
smashing right. The reason is obvious: In ordinary
position the left is ever so much quicker in its action,
having a.shorter distance to travel and going straight
instead of half-round, as the right must. In old days the
rule was never to use the right except at close-quarters.
Johnel s success over half-trained second-raters with his
marvellous right has ruined modern boxing. Jackson’s
style is the beau ideal of twenty years ago.

Whats der color er dis?” “Black,” the constable J

u.P another half-an-inch and slapping his chest with the I

colour . And then he sailed majestically on.
* # #
St. Hugo, Derby winner, is the biggest outsider
since Hermit.
* # *
For the first time in Derby history the House of
Commons rejected the usual motion for an adjournment
over the great race-day. Sir Wilfrid Lawson made his
point at last. He has been taking a division on the ques-
tion for many years, and increasing his little army of
followers at each attempt until now they have given the
old institution its death-blow. The House recognises the
Derby no more. The number of lords and dukes who run
horsesisgreaterthan.it ever was, but the turf stinks in
the nostrils of “ respectability ” and the glory of the Blue
Ribbon has departed.
• * * i
A sur-prize fight—The Jackson-Slavin contest. I
* # *
Too much parson, too much Cambridge, and too
much English don’t-you-knowness killed the (Queensland
eight in the intercolonial boat-race.
* * *
Lord Bradford, who won the Epsom Derby with
Sir Hugo, is one of the “mysterious” owners. His
horses are trained in comparative seclusion, and their
trials are snares for everybody but the “stable,” repre- j
sented by the trainer and one other at the most. Probably I
Bradford is allowed to.be in the know because he doesn’t !
“go talking,” but the men who pull the strings know’ a I
little more than his Ludship. The ordinary owner, as ;
everybody must understand, is usually the most pro-
foundly ignorant man on earth in matters connected with
his stud, and when he lias a horse proved to be above the
average, he delights in backing it heavily for aji the races
it doesn’t win. No dead certainties come from the Brad?
ford stable with a flourish of trumpets and get igno-
miniously beaten: They are horses that “ may be
included in the dangerous division,” and start at 20~ to 1
in big events. Frequently they are said “to -have been
doing badly at work,” and the worse they do before the
race the better they perform in public. Evidently the
British aristocracy didn’t get a very decided whisper
about Sir Hugo, who started a rank outsider in a small
field and upset Baron Hirsch’s “pot.” Wales and his
crowd would all be on the favourite, of course, and it is
improbable that any member of the Royal Family slapped
Bradford on the back after they had recovered from the
surprise.
Jack Slavm had better luck than his brother,
as he beat Con Riordan in 19 rounds in England on the
2nd inst. On his Australian form, Jack. Slavic was a rank
outsider.
* # *
Mr. Hill has not got another champion in Sham-
rock. He was genuinely backed by his party in the
Winter Stakes, and this remarkably clever party once
again “ fell in the soup,” as Shamrock was unable to show
up prominently.
• # «
Most of the sporting papers which tipped Slavin
to beat Jackson are taking their groats unkindly. It is
hard to see how they argue cut that Slavin was not
trained, seeing that he fought a stone lighter than
Jackson.
# # *
The N.S.W. Eight rowed a great race with luck
dead against them. One of the crew, to the writer’s
knowledge, was more fit to call in a doctor than pull a
race, and the positions gave Victoria an advantage of fully
a length.
• • •
They talk in England of prosecuting Jackson
and Slavin over their recent bout. Absurd. It was
known months beforehand that the mill was coming off
on a certain date, and if illegal the police who witnessed
the fight without interfering should be prosecuted as
accessories. However, as a British peer was amongst the
bottleholders, &c., it is not likely the prosecution will
take place.
• * •
Tattersall s Grand National Steeplechase, won
by Mr. “Petty” Gannon’s Grandwing, produced one of
the grandest races ever seen at Randwick, yet was not
responsible for a single accident. The win was popular.
Nolan secured the hurdles as well as the “steeple.”
Grand wing is the best steeplechaser on the Sydney side.
* • •
How soft Slavin was to admit, a few days after
the fight, that Jackson was the better man ! When he
blamed his had training for his defeat, several clubs set to
work to put up big prizes for a return match, but who
wants to see a light between Slavin and the man he pro-
claims to be his superior? By letting loose his tongue he
gave away thousands.
•• # *
The colour-line does not count for much in
Sydney, judging from the jubilation over Peter Jackson’s
win.
The way.in winch it was found out that foot-
bailer Webb was being paid to play with Geelong, was
that the suspicions of the residents were raised at the
enormous salary (£2 per week) given him. 1 Never before
had a shop-assistant in Geelong received: such a princely
screw. Had they started Him at £l'and gradually worked
up, no one would have interfered.
A ‘remarkable coincidence at the Birthday
Meeting, at Flemington, was that the-horses which won
the Hurdle, Royal Handicap and Steeplechase (Bolton
Sir John and Roll Roy) had each run second the week
previous at the Hunt Club Meeting, on the same course-
Bolton to Knight of the Garter ; Sir John to All There •’
and Rob Roy to Orangeman.

An advertisement for men to start the big
Melbourne sewerage job at Werribee caused about
1000 “unemployed”—mostly pick and shovelt
experts—to rush for the early morning train from
Spencer-street station the other morning. The
ordinary train under the new retrenchment regime
could barely accommodate the ordinary number o»£
passengers, and only a few of the unhappy
labourers, all of whom had paid Is. lid. each for
a ticket, were allowed to come on to the platform
and fight for standing room. Many hundreds of
them were shut out and told to wait five hours for
the next train, a special not being dreamt of in the
philosophy of the anti-Speight management. The
befooled navvies waited accordingly, and got
down to Werribee in time to find most of the first
detachment cursing their fate. Fifty men only
had been engaged. No extra train was provided
for the return journey, so the other 950 poor
fellows shivered on the Werribee platform for
hours and got home as best they could ! They had
contributed nearly <£loo in all to the railway
revenue, and been treated like dogs. The luckiest
of them picked up a few Werribee mushrooms for
consolation.

Higher Education.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When John Henty first came up to hospital he
was the personification of Church principles—his
Walk, his necktie, and the very laugh of the man
told you that he was a good churchman and a
firm believer in the creed of St. Athanasius. He
had rooms above mine and running down now and
then veneered us with a layer of his own respect-
ability. Men sitting about my room grew better
as he talked of his home, the old parsonage, pet
rabbits, and his sister. Charley Woods dropped
his “damns” and substituted “darn it,” and
Jefferies, the biggest rake in the college, donned a
Josephian purity of anecdote.
We met frequently without Henty and dis-
cussed the boy’s education, deciding ultimately by
a majority of one to leave him in the gentle hands
of Time and the omnipotent influence of his sur-
roundings.
Eighteen months of the coarser blasphemies of
a scientific education spoilt the charm of his clean
individuality, and I came back from Norway to
find him doubting the Immaculate Conception.
If you spoke of free will he would smile pitifully
and offer you a pamphlet.
The mere fact of another good-looking boy
placing his curly head beneath the wheels of the
juggernaut that Darwin started for the hawking of
doubts about the country would have been a small
matter had he not taken home with him the
cursed miasma of his doubts. But when I saw—for
I spent the long vacation with him —instead of the
old love-light that flew about the table in the
olden days from the sister, a wide-eyed dread for
the boy; from the mother a moister love for him,
and from the good clergyman’s self an anxious
watching for his prayers fulfilment, then, for the
first time, it dawned upon me how truly tragic it
was, from these people’s point of view, to be with-
out God in the world.
The end of Henty’s third year found him a
pagan of the new, or microscopic, order; he had
voluntarily relinquished all faith in the Infinitely
Great that he might have more time and energy to
devote to his new heritage, the Infinitely Little.
He now spelt God with a small “g,” and wore
dittoes on Sunday.
A prize offered by one of the professors for an
essay “ On the probable seat of the higher
moralities ” brought matters to a climax. I did
not see the boy for a fortnight. He sent me one
note by the servant saying, “ I shall not see you
for a few days ; I am going for the moralities
bald-headed 1”
One evening there came from his room the echo
of a mighty harangue, and I rushed up to find
Henty and the kitchen-maid. He had placed her
in his arm-chair, and with widely-dilated pupils
and hair on end was explaining to her his theory
of the fibrous interlacing that characterises the
two divisions of the medulla oblongata. The girl
was paralysed, and the boy was mad. I released
her, and he, taking up his essay, read to me as he
paced the room and gesticulated frantically 15
pages that comprised all that three years of
research had taught him, and which is still
regarded as the standard basis of mental research,
and two pages more of the wildest and most
inconsequent ranting; the line of demarcation
between the sense and nonsense marked the
moment of his brain-snapping.
Henty’s position as one of our most promising
students secured for him the constant attendance
of our staff. Each man came, stood over him,
examined his tongue, took his temperature, and
ordered him drugs, but still the patient raved, and
we all grew anxious.
One morning, contrary to the advice of his
many doctors, I telegraphed for his friends; they
came at nine—a consultation of all the specialists
had been arranged for ten, and I went up with
them. As the five of us entered the sick-room with
the cultured solemnity of our fraternity, a strange
picture presented itself. At the foot of the bed
stood the old clergyman and his wife, stooping
over the same book, the girl, with one hand in her
brother’s and the other holding her half of another
book which Henty shared, was leading the sing-
ing, and the hymn they sang was, “ Art thou
weary, art thou languid.” Tears were falling fast
from the old couple, the girl’s voice was half
tremulous, but quite strong, and the patient’s
quiet, bright face told us that he had gone back to
his boyhood and found his sanity.
The shock of our entry spoilt for a moment the
harmony, and the girl, out of respect for the
specialists, would have stopped the singing had
not the youngest and wisest among the doctors
motioned her to continue, and, taking at random
a handful of books from the table and giving one
to each of his confreres, pushed us into position
about the bedside. A capacity to sing hymns is
not common among University professors, and a
sense of decency only compelled our oculist to
bend reverently over Huxley’s handbook and
move his lips. The great authority on bones
peered into Haeckel, and was soon so lost to his
surroundings that he forgot to hum, while the
comparative biologist froWned and stared restively
from one to another, as though brought suddenly
into contact with a new and interesting species.
The singing over, Henty sank back upon his
pillow asleep, and the rest of us, with one excep-
tion, moved about the room uncomfortably—as
boys caught in the act of something mean.
The exception was the lecturer on bones, who
had found something in his book with which ho
did not agree, and the old war-horse was dead to
all else but the smell of battle from afar and the
hope of a future controversy.
The consultation that followed in my sitting-
room was a scene never to be forgotten. The boy
above, who had defied all treatment, and before
whose sleeplessness bromide and its allies had
failed ignominiously, was sleeping the sleep of
the just, and that, too, under a treatment that
had no written formula, nor was it included in
the columns of the British pharmacopoeia.
Hearing a movement outside, I went out, to
find the sister on the landing, and we waited there
until one of the doctors, with grim solemnity,
summoned us to hear the verdict.
The leading of the girl to her chair, the attitude
of the men as they gathered about her, was the
quintessence of professional formality.
“ We have been discussing your brother’s case,”
started the authority on mental diseases, under
whose special care Henty had been placed, “and
we have come to the conclusion that his pre-
sent condition is a somewhat serious one. For
sometime past I gather he has suffered from a
species of nerve-storm indicative of cerebral
hyperemia.”
“ In his letters to us,” the girl interjected, “he
has only spoken of headache.”
“ Headache, ah 1 well, that is but another name
for the disease to which I refer. Well, these
nerve-storms,-or headaches, as you call them, are
interesting to my colleagues and myself as sympto-
matic of a retrograde metamorphosis of nerve
element.” He took a deep breath, and with a
smile of self-satisfaction, asked if he made his
meaning clear. The girl bowed, and he continued :
“ This condition of things, Miss Henty, calls for
the exhibition of an alterative regime.” He looked
at his colleagues, and they smiled their acquies-
cence, “My friend, Dr. Holt, has examined the
patient’s retina, and although he finds a most
undoubted tendency to stamingspipilla, he is
inclined to think that the lesion is inorganic, and
once the balance between the choroid pressure and
the hoematomic tendency is re-established, the
apparent solution of continuity will disappear.
Our advice, then, Miss Henty, is that the altera-
tive treatment to which I referred, combined with
ozonic atmospheric surroundings, should be ”
“Might not we take him home and feed him
up!” the girl asked.
“Exactly,” exclaimed the youngest of the
fraternity; “take him home, give him eggs and
milk all day, lawn-tennis in the afternoon, and a
hymn"at bed-time,” and then they bowed them-
selves out, and the girl, turning to me, asked, in
tones full of anxiety, “Is Jack so very bad?”
seen upstairs, and it was not until I had explained

Together we went back to the bed-room, and
putting on the boy s dressing-gown the four of us
sat around the fire—talked birds’-nesting, fishing
days, and the village folk, until the ring of his old
laugh came back as wholesome as Naaman’s skin,
and the mother gladdened in her heart. Three
months later John Henty resigned his position as
a medical student, and the profession as a liveli-
hood. In a private letter to me, he said, lam
helping my father in his parish, and shall enter
the Church. Is it not strange,” he concluded,
“that my search for the seat of the higher mora-
lities should have been so successful ; for surely I
have found them in this quiet village, with its
sinlessness, my cricket club, and the romping of
our children about the seashore, while I yarn to
them of shells?'’ *
When at the end of the session the prizes
were distributed, my friend’s name was mentioned
for the last time. “A prize offered by myself for
an essay on the probable seat of the higher mora-
lities,” he said, speaking with evident emotion,
“ was won by John Henty, but I regret to say
that since then he has belied the promise of his
earlier student days, and matters culminated last
week when he severed his connection with the
University. The most charitable construction
that we can place upon his conduct is the supposi-
tion that the illness of which you may have heard
resulted in the deterioration of those higher brain
centres that are responsible for the growth of the
best intellectualities, and which it is our intention

MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
A Prouder Man than You.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
If you fancy that your parents came of better
stock than mine,
If you hint of higher breeding by a word or by a
sign ;
If you’re proud because of fortune, or the clever
things you do—
Then, I’ll play no second fiddle—l'm a prouder
man than you !
If you think that your profession has the more’
“ gentility,”
And that you are condescending to be seen along:
with me;
If you notice that I'm shabby while your clothes’
are spruce and new,
You have only got to hint it. I’m a prouder mam
than you 1
If you have a “swell ” companion when you sem
me on the street,
And you think that I’m too common for your
“ toney ” friend to meet,
So that I in passing closely fail to come within)
your view—
Then be blind to me for ever! I’m a prouder mam
than you 1
If your character is blameless—if your outward!
past is clean
While ’tis known my antecedents were not what
they should have been,
Do not risk contamination—save your name what-
e’er you do,
“Birds o’ feather fly together.” I’m a prouder
bird than you 1
Keep your patronage for others 1 Gold and station)
cannot hide
Friendship that can laugh at fortune, Friendship l
that can murder pride 1
Offer this as to an “equal,” let me see that yom
are true,
And my wall of pride is shatter’d —I am not so>
proud as you.
Sydney, April 23. ’92.
HENRY LAWSON.


Beware of Frauds. Drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.
“ I met Christie Murray, a few days ago,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent from London.
“He is one of the most picturesque - look-
ing men you could see in a week’s walk.
His hair is almost silvery white, and he
allows it to flow in graceful curls down to
his shoulders. He has been very ill with in-
fluenza, and still bears traces of it. He has just
returned from a short tour with “Ned’s Chum”
in Scotland, and reports a successful time. Little
Leo Byrne has made a great hit, and is pro-
nounced to be far away the best boy actor ever
seen in England in our day. Christie is taking
his company to some of the large provincial towns,
and expects to make money with his play. He
recently sold to a clever Midland speculator the
serial rights of his story, “Bob Martin’s
Daughter,” for =£7oo. He finds now to his disgust
that the smart Midlander has already made £l7OO
out of his bargain.”
Wolfe’s Schnapps. —Sternly refuse imitations.

A Night with a “Hatter."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I have often Heard of men’s hair turning white
in a single night through terror, but I don’t
believe it. Anyhow, my hair didn’t change colour
that night, and 1 reckon I went through enough
to whiten the head of a nigger.
I was coming down one of the Seven Creeks in
the Strathbogie ranges one cold, rainy evening,
looking for a place to camp, when just at the foot
of one of those long bills 1 sighted a big gum-tree
all rotten and burnt out, but still standing. It was
just large enough inside for one man, and seemed
to have been well patronised, judging from the
snug bed of dry boughs and leaves inside, and the
large heap of ashes just in front. I threw my
swag inside, for it had settled into a steady rain,
got a good fire going and a heap of wood for the
night, and then went down to the creek for a billy
of water.
When I came back I was not overjoyed to find
another swag beside mine in the tree, and the
proprietor of it bringing a big log for the fire as a
hint that he meditated camping there that night.
I spoke civilly enough to him but I thought to
myself there would be trouble at turn-in time. He
must have perceived it was only large enough
for one, and I could see he had no tent. As soon
as I had finished my tea and damper I unrolled
my swag and lay down full length in the dis-
puted territory, remembering that possession
is nine points of the law. To my surprise,
ho took no notice of this John-Bullish pro-
ceeding—ho just squared off in front of
the fire and began talking to himself, scarcely
loud enough for me to hear. Now, although I was
a now-chum to the bush, I had often heard of mad
swagsmon, and knew they were generally harmless
creatures who had gone cranky through carrying
bluey over hot scorching plains from one year’s
end to another in the now almost hopeless
search for work. But somehow I didn’t like the
gleam in his eyes when I found them fastened on
me once or twice with a wild, hunted look in them.
I now looked him over, carefully noting that he
was a big, strapping six-foot man, which fact,
along with his eye-glare, made me think posses-
sion might not mean so many points, after all.
With these things in view 1 tried to draw him
into a pleasant conversation when I found to my
alarm that he would talk of nothing but murder.
My efforts to start an argument with him over
Henry George’s theory fell flat. Even my forcible
remarks about General Booth failed to interest
him. I have often felt more satisfied and at peace
with everybody than I did when ho came and sat
down just at my feet out of the rain, and began
to rub his great, long sheath-knife with a small
stone, at the same time remarking that, talking
about murders, ho believed many a man had been
killed in these ranges, and no one was ever the
wiser of it. I lost no time in assuring him that I
didn’t believe a man had ever killed another yet
■without being caught and hanged. I even grew
eloquent on the fact that murder will out, and
gave several instances of it which had come under
my own observation. Some of these facts were a
groat strain on my imagination, but I kept at it. I
also dwelt at some length on the almost miraculous
sagacity of our detectives, not forgetting the
wonderful native black-tracker, who never loses
a track once he starts on it. Then I pointed out
how the bushrangers had all been run down,
despite the fact that they were expert bushmen,
after which I tried to drift in an offhand, uncon-
cerned way on to the Home Rule question, as I
thought I had noticed some brogue in his talk.
But he took no interest whatever in the fact that
I would like to see England blown to pieces some
dark night, so that the Irish people could govern
themselves.
“ Look here, mate,” he said, leaning over me,
and feeling the edge of his knife, “you can talk
as you like about crimes being found out, but I’ve
done some things in my time which I wouldn’t
want to have found out, I can tell you. I’ll tell
you a thing I did once; it will ease my mind a
bit, but I warn you if you let it out, I’ll haunt
you to your grave.”
I intimated that I always respected the confi-
dence of a friend.
“Mo and Dick Jones went up this very creek
years ago, away back in them dark ranges, where
white man had never been. I didn’t treat Dick
square either. You see, I’ll tell you how it was.
We followed this creek up for two weeks, pros-
pectin’ here and there, till at last we struck
payable ground. Then Dick he takes the two
packhorses and goes back for rations, leavin’ me
to look after the claim. Well, lie comes back
in about ten days, and, paralyse mo, if lie
didn’t bring his wife and two boys with him.
Queer place to fetch a woman, wasn’t it ? We was
getting on right enough till one Sunday I hap-
pened to go up on the ledge of rock above Dick’s
tent to see if I could see any smoke down the
creek. Of course we didn’t want our place to bo
found out, and I noticed the great flat rock which
hung over Dick’s tent wasvery loose. You see, Dick
had rigged a sort of house-tent close against the
bottom of the ledge so as to get under the shelter
of the overhanging rock. It must have hung out
nearly 20ft. over the ledge. While I stood looking
down a sudden thought struck me—‘Why divide
that gold ?’ There was enough in that claim to
make one man rich. Well, to make a long story
short, that night about twelve I creeps out of my
bark humpy, gets a long sapling for a lever, and
sneaks round on to the ledge. I got the sapling in
between it and the main rock and found I could
shift it easy. Then I goes and looks down.
Everything was very quiet. There was the white
roof of the tent showing among the scrub and the
fire just dying out. Then I gives one great heave
and that big rock fell flat on Dick’s tent. They
never knew what struck ’em. That rock weighed
hundreds of tons. Well, I stood watching the
fire a minute and then I goes down to cover all
signs of the crime and —but I’m keeping you
awake, mate, so I think I’ll just shove on another
billy of water. I always like a drink of hot tea
before turning in. Take no notice if you hear me
going about in the night—l’m a terror to walk in
ray sleep. What? Take your bed. Oh, no, I’ll make
my doss down at the back of this tree. A little
rain won’t hurt an old hand like me, only some-
times it makes me feel queer in the head.”
Then he took his billy and went down towards
the stream. If ever I rolled my swag quicker
than I did that time, the occasion has escaped my
memory. I walked till near morning, and was
wet to the skin when I sighted an old splitter’s
hut. I found four men asleep inside, but they all
roused up when they found I was wet through.
When I told them the way I had come, they asked
me if I had missed the hollow tree. I told them I
had camped there, but—-here they all roared out
laughing, and sung out all together “How did he
shift yon ?” I was a lot puzzled till one of them
explained :
“ How did you and Jim Carney get on ? He is
a hard case, and no mistake. You see, he left
here last night, as he reckoned he would reach the
old hollow-tree before it set in to rain. I told him
to stop here, as he might find someone already
camped there, but he said he was in a hurry to get
into Mansfield, and, anyhow, ho said if he found
anyone camped there he would shift him, if he
weighed a ton. So he shifted you, did he ? How
did he manage it ? ’
I gravely informed them that I had been too
well brought up to deprive an older man of his
bod, on a wet night especially. Then I went on
drying my clothes. Some people are too curious
to live.
BOILING BILLY.
Featherstonhaugh.*
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Brookong station lay half-asleep
Dozed in the waning western glare,
’Twas before the run had been stocked with sheep
And only cattle depastured there,
As the Blueeap mob reined up at the door
And loudly saluted Featherstonhaugh.
“ My saintly preacher,” the leader cried,
“ I stand no nonsense, as you’re aware,
I’ve a word for you if you’ll step outside,
Just drop that pistol and have a care,
I’ll trouble you, too, for the key of the store,
For we’re short of tucker, friend Featherston-
haugh.”
The muscular Christian showed no fear,
Though he handed the key with but small delay;
He never answered the ruffian’s jeer
Except by a look which seemed to say—
“ Beware, my friend, and think twice before
You raise the devil in Featherstonhaugh.”
Two hours after he reined his horse
Up in Urana, and straightway went
To the barracks, the trooper was gone, of course,
Blindly nosing a week-old scent
Away in the scrub around Mount Galore.
“ Confound the fellow!” quoth Featherstonhaugh.
“ Will any man of you come with me
And give this Blueeap a dressing-down?”
They all regarded him silently
As he turned his horse, with a scornful frown.
“You’re curs, the lot of you, to the core—
I’ll go by myself,” said Featherstonhaugh.
The scrub was thick on Urangeline,
As he followed the tracks that twisted through
The box and dogwood and scented pine
(One of their horses had cast a shoe).
Steeped from his youth in forest lore.
He could track like a nigger, could Featherstou-
haugh.
He paused as he saw the thread of smoke
From the outlaw camp, and ho marked the
sound
Of a hobble-check, as it sharply broke
The silence that held the scrub land bound.
There were their horses—two, three, four—
“lt’s a risk, but I’ll chance it!” quoth Feathcroton-
haugh.
He loosed the first, and it walked away,
But his comrade’s silence could not be bought,
For he raised his head with a sudden neigh,
And plainly showed that he’d not be caught.
As a bullet sang from a rifle-bore—
“lt’s time to be moving,” quoth Fcatherston
hangh.
The brittle pine, as they broke away,
Crackled like ice in a winter’s ponds.
The strokes fell fast on the cones that lay
Buried beneath the withered fronds
That softly carpet the sandy floor—
Swept two on the tracks of Featherstonhaugh.
They struck the path that the stock had made,
A dustily-red, well-beaten track,
The leader opened a fusillade
Whose target was Featherston’s stooping back
But his luck was out, not a bullet tore
As much as a shred from Featherstonhaugh.
Rattle ’em, rattle ’em fast on the pad,
Where the sloping shades fell dusk and dim ;
The manager’s heart beat high and glad,
For he knew the creek was a mighty swim.
Already he heard a smothered roar—
“ They’re done like a dinner!” quoth Featherston-
haugh.
It was almost dark as they neared the dam ;
He struck the crossing as true as a hair;
For the space of a second the pony swam,
Then shook himself in the chill night air.
In a pine-tree shade on the further shore,
With his pistol cocked, stood Featherstonhaugh
A splash—an oath—and a rearing horse.
A thread snapped short in the fateful loom,
The tide, unaltered, swept on its course
Though a fellow creature had met his doom,
Pale and trembling, and struck with awe,
Blueeap stood opposite Featherstonhaugh.
While the creek rolled muddily in between
r The eddies played with the drowned man’s hat.
The stars peeped out in the summer sheen,
A night-bird chirruped across the flat —
Quoth Blueeap, “ I owe you a heavy score,
And I’ll live to repay it, Featherstonhaugh.”
But he never did, for he ran his race
Before he had time to fulfil his oath,
I can’t think how, but, in any case,
He was hung, or drowned, or maybe both.
But whichever it was, he came no more
To trouble the peace of Featherstonhaugh.
BARCROET H. BOAKE.

Pure blood is a life-long delight. It is health—real good health and
strength—free from pimples, spots, sores and aches.
Have pure blood by taking Prazer’s Sulphur Tablets internally, and use
a leally good modern soap—Frazer’s Sulphur Soap—externally, and you
ensure a clear skin and a good complexion.
It is a mistake to suppose that a good skin or a clear complexion can be
secured by Soap alone. It cannot. A. pure Soap—Frazer’s Sulphur Soap
is an invaluable aid to personal beauty, but the first essential is pure blood.
I his must never be forgotten. If we can’t have the blood quite pure, then
we must get it as pure as we can,
A man wants a clear skin and a wholesome complexion, in profession or
business. Else if he be blotchy, pimply, or disfigured, other men fight shy
of dealing with him. Let the man take Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets internally
and use Frazer’s Sulphur Soap externally,
A woman needs a good complexion else her happiness is decreased, and

Long Jim’s Love Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ ‘ When Shadders Build ’; that’s my song,” said
Jim, as he gazed dreamily into the lire. It was a
fine night, after two of storm and rain, and the
cattle, tired out by two restless camps, were sleep-
ing soundly on a dry sand-ridge. Long Jim was
the bullock-driver, and was a living example that
the bullock-driver is born, not made. To see him
straighten up a team on a bad pinch was to wit-
ness a work of high art. There was no raving
and swearing accompanied by a storm of whip-
cracks, but by some magic impulse the beasts
wore touched up to the right degree, and then,
all together, with one mighty heave, something
had to come.
“ ‘ When Sparrows Build,’ I suppose you mean,”
said the cook.
“ Ah, it might be when sparrers build, but I
didn’t get the right hang of the words—it was the
way it was sung fetched me.”
“ Where did you hear it sung, Jim ?” I asked.
“ Down in Port Swan just before we started.”
“It was a gal sang it, I suppose?” said the
cook as he piled a shovelful of hot embers on to the
top of the camp-oven.
Jim sighed deeply in affirmation : he was a
taciturn man and never spoke more than he could-
help.
That was the first I heard of Jim’s love story.
We had a long trip, and after delivering the
cattle, I returned by steamer; Jim came down as
well and we both disembarked at Port Swan. On
the second day, Jim came to the hotel where I
was staying and sheepishly asked me if I would go
down to where he was putting up and have a
drink ? I had almost forgotten about the girl who
sang “ When Sparrows Build,” but something in
Jim’s manner brought it to my mind, and I
guessed I was about to see the damsel.
She was behind the bar, and it was with much
pride Jim asked me what I would partake of.
“ Poor Jim,” I thought when I looked at her.
The remains of a once fine woman, one who had
come down step by step until she had to earn a
living as barmaid in a second-rate pub. in a little
seaport. Poor Jim !
Jim saw nothing of this ; to him the little arts
and smiles, that in a young girl would have been
appropriate and charming, were quite real. He
accepted her fascinations at her own valuation,
and was supremely happy. I knew Jim was a
quiet fellow who had saved a bit of money ; but it
was impossible to wafn him in any way—he was
in the toils. Before I left Port Swan Jim got
married, and as I went away out back I did not
hear of him for some time.
Two years afterwards as I was riding towards
B , I overtook two bullock-teams, and thought I
recognised the lanky figure with the leading team.
Sure enough it was Jim. He was delighted to
see me, but when I asked after the wife I received
only an evasive reply, I camped with the teams
that night, and his mate told me what I expected
—that she had made Jim’s money fly and then
insisted on going out as barmaid again, as she
couldn’t bear being left by herself when he was
away on the roads.
“She’s keeping straight enough,” went on my
informant, “at the same time it would be a
deuced good thing for Jim if she did take up with
some other fellow and clear out.”
Evidently the girl who sang “ When Sparrows
Build ” was not popular amongst Jim’s friends.
“ Can you give me some quinine, my mate’s
awful bad with fever.”
It was on the road to the Kimberley. The speaker
had come from a camp just off the road, and
stopped us as wo were passing. It was noon, so
we turned out for a spell and I went over to see
if I could do anything for the sick man. Gaunter
and leaner than ever, there lay poor Jim, shaking
with ague, under a pile of blankets. We stopped
there that night, and next day Jim was better,
but he told mo nothing about himself, or how he
came to be seeking a fortune on Kimberley. We
had to go on, but I promised Jim to keep a look-
out for him up there if we should be at the same
camp. I saw Jim several times during the
ensuing months, but ho never said a word about
the past. .
“ There’s a pub. started at the 18-mile camp,”
said one of the fellows one evening. “The
fellow who is running it has got his “wife”
with him. Had a good voice once, and does a bit
of singing now.”
“Good enough for the place, I suppose,’ said
one of the others.
“Yes, she was grinding out ‘When Sparrows
Build ’ the night I was in there.”
Jim was smoking at our fire that evening, but
he made no remark, and I was not surprised to
hear the next day that he had gone over to the
18-mile. I was a little bit uncomfortable, for if
his wife had run away and this was she, why,
there’s no knowing what a quiet man will do some-
times.
Next day, however, he turned up smiling.
“ It’s ail right,” he said to me confidentially.
“ What is ? ” I asked in surprise.
“Why, about my wife. It was her right enough,
but I frightened the d%vil out of the pair of them,
and I got this,” and he produced a piece of paper.
It ran as follows “ I hereby give up all right and
title in my husband, James Strickland, and we
mutually agree to leave each other alone for the
future. (Signed) Agnes Strickland.”
Naturally I roared with laughter. “ Why,
Jim,” I said, “ what’s the good of this ?”
“ Why, she said I could get married again, and
that she could do the same, and that she had no
claim on me for money or anything after signing
this.” .
“Rubbish, Jim, you are as much married as
ever. You would get a divorce without much
trouble I expect; but this paper is only humbug,
and she knew it.”
“ Well I’m hanged, and I wanted to clear out
back to Queensland and settle down. Now I’ve
stood a lot; she spent all my money, she ran
off with this fellow, and now she’s trying to make
a fool of me, but I’m full.”
Jim went away muttering, and three days after-
wards I heard that there had been a fearful com-
motion at the 18-mile. Jim and his mates
had rolled up and half killed the shanty keeper,
and burnt the place down ; there were no police
up there, and the fellows all enjoyed the sport.
Jim’s patience was exhausted. Shortly after he
returned to Queensland,-and when next I saw him
I was able to tell him that he was really a widower,
as his wife was dead of fever. He is married
again, but his wife does not sing. Jim says he
hates such nonsense.
DELCOMYN.
The Anglican Bishop of Exeter has ad-
ministered the rite of confirmation to a number
of the inmates of an asylum for idiots. Confirmed
idiots !
The daily newspapers allowed their imagin-
ations to run riot on the subject of Deeming in
prison. His alleged autobiographies, so full of
lurid confessions and startling tales of adventure,
were no more than a series of drivelling reitera-
tions of his disconnected “speech ” from the dock,
interspersed with doggerel rhyme and obscenity.
He never regarded the only book supplied to him
(a large, seedy-looking volume with “ Church of
England Chaplain’s Bible” inscribed on the fly-
leaf) as other than a dull substitute for a Zolaian
novel, and ho steadfastly refused to talk about his
future until he couldn’t get anybody to join him
in conversation about anything else. The “ two
warders, one on either side, guarding him night
and day in his cell” were a journalistic fiction.
He didn’t have anybody in his cell except himself.
Three warders were told off, in the usual way, to
take turns at sitting outside'the iron gate of his
apartment within a recess that is a decidedly
tight fit for one at a time.
The last daily paper story about the Dora-
Dora blacks has just gone bung, and the bottom
has fallen out of it. According to this latest
Ananias the pair were travelling in Queensland
when one of them was chewed by a snalce and dis-
abled. Then the pair either climbed down or fell
down an abandoned shaft, or a hole, or someplace,
and either hid tliere or weren’t able to get out.
Anyhow, a white man found them —one dying and
the other in good health and ready for new
atrocities —and was just going to capture the
pair, or to run away, or something, when
a gun was placed against his head and lie
had to put his hands up. Then a contribution ot
victuals was levied upon him—either he was
carrying the victuals or else the healthy
black made him go and get them ; and
after that the principal aborigine left him and the
sick nigger together, and struck out for parts un-
known with the provender. This story held good
for several days, and then a short, dry paragraph
appeared stating that it was all lies—the snake
included, and the legendary pair had neither been
seen nor heard of. No explanation was offered as
to the origin of the tale. At latest advices there
reason to believe that one of the Dora Dora
blacks is about to start-a paper in Sydney, and the
other one is to be made a K.O.M.C.
A London publishing authority v;ilues
Spurgeon’s copyrights, including 500 unpublished
sermons, at =£50,000.

is taking up the question cf imported hog wash
periodicals of the cheap, nasty and sweated type.

Haynes, Deeming’s attorney after bis arrest,
admits that Deeming did make a confession of
murder to him. People are asking bow this
squares with Mr. Haynes’ former strenuous
denial.
